Modality Transfer Proof via Khalf
Author proves by contradiction that if one term is more general, its modality must apply to the specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof that the modality of a predicate must be the same for a general term and its specific instance.

Core Idea
Modality cannot differ between general and specific without contradiction.

In modal logic, a key principle is that if a predicate holds necessarily for a general term, it must also hold necessarily for any specific term under it. Conversely, if it holds only possibly for a specific, it cannot be necessary for the general. The author here provides a rigorous proof by contradiction (khalf) showing that assuming otherwise leads to an absurdity. This lesson walks through the two-case proof, reinforcing the importance of modal consistency in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Assumption to be Refuted
The proof starts by assuming that the modality of a predicate (j) differs between two terms (a and b) where one is more general than the other. For example, j is necessary for b but only possible for a, or vice versa.
The assumption is the denial of the principle being proved.
2
Enumerate the Two Possible Generality Relations
Either b is more general than a (a is under b), or a is more general than b (b is under a). The proof treats each case separately.
These two cases cover all possibilities given that one is more general.
3
Follow the First Case (b under a)
If b is under a (a more general), then the modality of j relative to a must transfer to b. But if j is necessary for b and only possible for a, that violates the transfer. Hence a contradiction.
The contradiction is between 'j is necessary for b' and 'j is only possible for a' because the general's modality must apply to the specific.
4
Follow the Second Case (a under b)
If a is under b (b more general), then the modality of j from b transfers to a. If j is necessary for b, it must be necessary for a. But the assumption says j is only possible for a, again a contradiction.
This mirrors the first case, swapping the roles.
5
Conclude the Proof
Both cases lead to contradiction, so the original assumption (that modality can differ) is false. Therefore, the modality must be the same for the general and the specific.
This confirms the principle of modal transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرورة أنه ضروري لمساويه وهو أيضاً خلف، وإن كان أحدهما أعم من الآخر، والآخر داخل في عمومه فإما أن يكون "ب" داخلاً تحت "أ" أو "أ" داخلاً تحت "ب" فإن كان الأول فيلزم أن يكون "ج" منسوباً إلى "ب" بجهة نسبته إلى "أ" لكون "ب" داخلة تحت "أ" ومن ضرورة ثبوت الجهة لأعمّ أمرين ثبوتها لأخصهما وهو خلف، فإن كانت جهة نسبة "ج" إلى "ب" بالضرورة، وجهة نسبته إلى "أ" بالإمكان الخاص، وإن كان "أ" داخلاً تحت "ب" فيلزم أن تكون جهة نسبة "ج" إليه هي جهة نسبته إلى "ب" لما حققناه، وقد كان جهة نسبته إلى "ب" بالضرورة، فتكون جهة نسبته إلى "أ" بالضرورة، وقد كان بالإمكان وهو خلف.
Necessity that it is necessary for its equal, which is also a contradiction. If one of them is more general than the other, and the other is included within its generality, then either 'b' is under 'a' or 'a' is under 'b'. If the first, then it follows that 'j' is related to 'b' with the same modality as its relation to 'a' because 'b' is under 'a', and from the necessity of establishing the modality for the more general of two things comes its establishment for the more specific, which is a contradiction. So if the modality of the relation of 'j' to 'b' is necessary, and its relation to 'a' is specific possibility, [contradiction]. And if 'a' is under 'b', then it follows that the modality of the relation of 'j' to it is the same as its relation to 'b' according to what we have verified, and its relation to 'b' was necessary, so its relation to 'a' becomes necessary, whereas it was possible, which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction (خلف) regarding the transfer of modality from a more general term to a more specific term. The author sets up two possibilities: either term 'b' is under the generality of 'a', or 'a' is under the generality of 'b'. In the first case, if 'j' is related to 'a' with a certain modality (e.g., necessary), then because 'b' is more specific, 'j' must also be related to 'b' with the same modality; however, if the text assumes that the relation to 'b' is necessary but to 'a' is only possible (specific possibility), that contradicts the principle that what holds for the more general must hold for the more specific. Similarly, if 'a' is under 'b', then if 'j' is necessary for 'b', it should be necessary for 'a' as well, but if it was assumed possible for 'a', a contradiction arises. Thus, the author shows that the modal relation cannot differ between a general term and its specific subterm without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if 'j' is necessarily related to 'b' and 'b' is more general than 'a', what must be the relation of 'j' to 'a'?
A) Necessarily related
B) Possibly related
C) Impossible
D) Not determined

Key Takeaway
The modality of a predicate must remain constant when moving from a general term to its specific subterm; otherwise, a logical contradiction arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Use the khalf method to prove that if the predicate 'created' is necessary for 'the world', it must also be necessary for every part of the world (e.g., mountains, rivers). Assume it is only possible for a part and derive a contradiction.
?
2. In a fiqh discussion, a jurist argues that 'riba' is haram with necessity, but claims that a specific type of transaction (e.g., credit card interest) is only possibly haram. Apply the modal transfer proof to show this position leads to contradiction.

Reductio ad Absurdum in Modal Syllogisms
Author describes reductio method for modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the reductio method to test the validity of a modal syllogism by deriving a contradiction.

Core Idea
Reductio assumes the contradictory of the conclusion to expose inconsistency in the premises.

In modal logic, not every syllogism can be proven by direct conversion. The reductio method offers an alternative: assume the negation of the desired conclusion, combine it with the premises, and deduce a contradiction. This lesson unpacks the classical Islamic logical procedure for reductio as described by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (al-matloob)
Determine which proposition you intend to prove from the given premises. This is the conclusion of the modal syllogism under investigation.
2
Assume its contradictory (naqid al-matloob)
Take the opposite proposition: if the conclusion is universal affirmative, assume the particular negative, etc. This assumption becomes a new premise.
The contradictory must exactly oppose the conclusion in quality, quantity, and modality.
3
Place this assumption as the minor premise with the original major premise
Combine the assumed contradictory (as minor) with the original major premise (as major) to form a new syllogism.
The author specifies that the contradictory should be made the minor premise.
4
Deduce from this pair the contradictory of the original minor premise
If the new syllogism is valid, it must yield a conclusion that contradicts the original minor premise. This contradiction shows that the original conclusion cannot be false.
The deduction relies on the original modal relationships.
5
Verify that this contradiction establishes the original conclusion
Since assuming the opposite leads to an impossibility (contradiction among the premises), the original conclusion must be true. This completes the reductio proof.
The method is valid only if the contradictory assumption leads to a genuine contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البيان بطريق الخلف فلا بد إذا أخذت نقيض المطلوب في الكل وجعلته صغرى للكبرى أنتج نقيض المقدمة الصغرى. وأما البيانات الأخرى من العكس والافتراض وأن تبين بها إنتاج بعض هذه الاقترانات فليس بمطلق في الجميع. أما العكس فإنه إن أمكن أن يبين به ما مثل الاقتران الثاني بعكس الصغرى منه، فليس يمكن ذلك في ما سواه. أما الأول فلأنك إن عكست الصغرى عادت جزئية والكبرى لا عكس لها؛ لكونها ممكنة. وأما الثالث فلأنك أن عكست الصغرى إما أن تبقيها صغرى بحالها أو تجعلها كبرى، فإن أبقيتها صغرى فالاقتران يكون من الشكل الرابع لا من الشكل الأول، وإن جعلتها كبرى فهي جزئية ولا قياس في الشكل الأول عما كبراه جزئية، والكبرى فلا تنعكس؛ لكونها ممكنة. وأما الرابع فلما سبق. وأما الخامس فلأنك إن عكست الصغرى فهو غير مفيد؛ لما مضى في الثالث وإن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية والجزئي لا ينتج الكلي، ثم أما أن تبقيها كبرى أو تجعلها صغرى فإن كان الأول فهي جزئية وشرط الشكل الأول أن تكون كبراه كليّة، وإن جعلتها صغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الشكل الرابع. وأما السادس فلأنه وإن عاد إلى الأول بعكس إحدى مقدمتيه فهما سالبتان ولا قياس في الشكل الأول عن سالبتين. وأما السابع فلأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية ولا قياس عن جزئيتين، وإن عكست الصغرى فهو غير مفيد؛ لما عرف في الاقتران الثالث. وأما الثامن فلأنك إن عكست الصغرى فهي غير مفيدة؛ لما عرف في الاقتران الرابع والكبرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها ممكنة سالبة، وعلى هذا فلو كانت الكبرى ضرورية والصغرى ممكنة كان ما يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول الاقتران الأول والثالث، بعكس الكبرى منهما كما سلف ولا يتبين بالعكس ما سواهما. وأما الثاني فلأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، فإن أبقيتها كبرى فلا إنتاج من الشكل الأول، وإن جعلتها صغرى عاد إلى الشكل الرابع وما بعده فلما عرف. وأما الافتراض فلا يبين به غير الاقتران الرابع على ما بيناه، وما سواه فلا ينتظم من لوازم الشيء المفروض فيها اقتران مفض إلى إنتاج المطلوب أصلاً، واعتبره تجده كذلك.
As for demonstration by the method of reductio (khalf), it is necessary that when you take the contradictory of the desired conclusion in the major premise and make it the minor premise of the major, it yields the contradictory of the minor premise. As for the other demonstrations by conversion (aks) and assumption (iftirad), and that by them the productivity of some of these conjunctions is shown, it is not absolute for all. As for conversion, if it can be used to show the second conjunction by converting its minor, that is not possible for others. As for the first, because if you convert the minor it becomes particular, and the major has no conversion because it is possible. As for the third, because if you convert the minor, either you keep it as minor or make it major; if you keep it as minor, the conjunction becomes of the fourth figure, not the first; and if you make it major, it is particular, and there is no syllogism in the first figure whose major is particular, and the major does not convert because it is possible. As for the fourth, due to what preceded. As for the fifth, because if you convert the minor it is useless due to what passed in the third, and if you convert the major it becomes particular, and the particular does not yield the universal; then either you keep it as major or make it minor: if the first, it is particular and the condition of the first figure is that its major be universal; if you make it minor, the conjunction reverts to the fourth figure. As for the sixth, because even if it reverts to the first by converting one of its premises, then both are negative and there is no syllogism in the first figure from two negatives. As for the seventh, because if you convert the major it becomes particular and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and if you convert the minor it is useless as known in the third conjunction. As for the eighth, because if you convert the minor it is useless as known in the fourth conjunction, and the major has no conversion because it is possible and negative. On this basis, if the major were necessary and the minor possible, then what is shown by conversion and reduction to the first figure is the first and third conjunctions, by converting their major as previously, and what other than them is not shown by conversion. As for the second, because if you convert the major it becomes particular; if you keep it as major, there is no production from the first figure; if you make it minor, it reverts to the fourth figure and what follows, as known. As for assumption, it does not show other than the fourth conjunction, as we explained; and other than it, from the concomitants of the assumed thing, there does not generally compose a conjunction leading to the production of the desired conclusion, and consider it and you will find it so.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage explains the methods for demonstrating that certain modal syllogisms (conjunctions of premises) are productive. The author first describes the reductio method: assume the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with the major premise to derive the contradictory of the minor. Then he turns to conversion (عكس) and assumption (افتراض). He lists eight conjunctions (presumably of modal premises) and analyzes which can be shown valid by conversion, explaining why each fails or succeeds based on figure conditions and modal properties. For example, the first conjunction cannot be shown by converting the minor because that makes it particular, and the major (possible) cannot be converted. The second can be shown by converting its minor? Actually he says 'the second' is possible by converting the minor, but later he says for the second if you convert the major it becomes particular. There is some inconsistency; perhaps the text means different things. The author then notes that only the first and third conjunctions can be shown by conversion when the major is necessary and the minor possible. Finally, he states that assumption only works for the fourth conjunction. The overall lesson is that not all mixed modal syllogisms can be reduced to the first figure by conversion; only some are convertible.

نقيض
مطلوب
صغرى
كبرى

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the reductio method produce when you take the contradictory of the desired conclusion as the minor premise with the major premise?
A) The contradictory of the major premise
B) The contradictory of the minor premise
C) The original conclusion
D) A new conclusion unrelated to the premises

Key Takeaway
Reductio is a powerful tool for modal syllogisms that cannot be proven by conversion alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Use the reductio method to show that the syllogism 'Every A is necessarily B; some C is possibly A; therefore some C is possibly B' is valid. Write out the steps as the author describes: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine with the major premise, and derive a contradiction with the minor premise.

Conversion in Modal Syllogisms: When It Works and When It Fails
Author analyzes conversion method for modal conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which modal conjunctions can be reduced to the first figure by conversion and which cannot, based on conditions of figure and modality.

Core Idea
Conversion is not a universal key; only specific modal conjunctions can be proven by conversion.

The author examines eight conjunctions of modal premises and tests each for convertibility. The results reveal strict conditions: conversions must preserve universality, avoid negative pairs, and respect figure restrictions. This lesson walks through the reasoning for each case as presented in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight conjunctions of modal premises
The author refers to eight iqtiranat (conjunctions) of modal premises. These are combinations of necessary, possible, affirmative, and negative premises in the first figure. Identify which conjunction is being tested (e.g., first, second, etc.).
The exact list is not given in this passage, but the author refers to them sequentially.
2
Identify the conversion operation applied
Determine which premise is being converted: the minor or the major. Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth and adjusting quantity if needed.
Possible propositions cannot be converted (the author states 'the major has no conversion because it is possible').
3
Check the resulting premises for first-figure conditions
After conversion, check if the major premise remains universal (the first figure requires a universal major) and if the premises are not both negative or both particular.
If conversion makes the major particular, or creates a pair of negatives, the syllogism is invalid.
4
Determine if conversion leads to a different figure
If converting the minor and keeping it as minor yields a fourth-figure syllogism, it is not a valid reduction to the first figure. Similarly, if the converted premise becomes major and is particular, the first figure condition fails.
The author mentions the fourth figure as a problematic outcome.
5
Conclude whether conversion proves the conjunction
Only if after conversion the premises satisfy the first figure conditions (universal major, not both negative/particular) does conversion prove the original conjunction. The author finds only the first and third conjunctions meet this when major is necessary and minor possible.
The passage gives detailed failures for each of the eight.

Reduction Constraints to the First Figure
Author explains reduction failures for sixth-eighth conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conjunctions of a categorical syllogism cannot be reduced to the first figure and explain the specific obstacle (negative premises, particular premises, or conversion impossibility).

Core Idea
Not every syllogism reduces to first figure – quality and quantity can block conversion.

In this passage, the author systematically examines why certain conjunctions (sixth, seventh, eighth) of a categorical syllogism cannot be reduced to the first figure. He shows that conversion may lead to two negative premises, two particular premises, or a premise that cannot convert at all. Understanding these restrictions is essential for evaluating whether a syllogistic proof in kalam or fiqh is valid and reducible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conjunction numbers
The author refers to 'the sixth', 'the seventh', and 'the eighth' conjunctions. Determine which figure these refer to (likely the fourth figure, based on previous context).
The passage assumes a classification of conjunctions within a given figure.
2
Check conversion outcome for each
For the sixth: converting one premise leaves both negative. For the seventh: converting the major gives a particular; converting the minor is useless (third conjunction). For the eighth: converting the minor is useless (fourth conjunction) and the major (possible negative) cannot convert.
Recall that possible negatives do not convert (only affirmative universals convert to particulars).
3
Apply first-figure conditions
First figure requires: major universal, minor affirmative, and at least one affirmative. Two negatives or two particulars are invalid. Also the major must convert if needed.
4
Summarize which produce and which do not
Only the first and third conjunctions (when major is necessary and minor possible) reduce by conversion; the others do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية كليّة، وإن جعلتها صغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الشكل الرابع. وأما السادس فلأنه وإن عاد إلى الأول بعكس إحدى مقدمتيه فهما سالبتان ولا قياس في الشكل الأول عن سالبتين. وأما السابع فلأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية ولا قياس عن جزئيتين، وإن عكست الصغرى فهو غير مفيد؛ لما عرف في الاقتران الثالث. وأما الثامن فلأنك إن عكست الصغرى فهي غير مفيدة؛ لما عرف في الاقتران الرابع والكبرى فلا عكس لها؛ لكونها ممكنة سالبة، وعلى هذا فلو كانت الكبرى ضرورية والصغرى ممكنة كان ما يتبين بالعكس والرد إلى الشكل الأول الاقتران الأول والثالث، بعكس الكبرى منهما كما سلف ولا يتبين بالعكس ما سواهما. أما الثاني فلأنك إن عكست الكبرى عادت جزئية، فإن أبقيتها كبرى فلا إنتاج من الشكل الأول، وإن جعلتها صغرى عاد إلى الشكل الرابع وما بعده فلما عرف. وأما الافتراض فلا يبين به غير الاقتران الرابع على ما بيناه، وما سواه فلا ينتظم من لوازم الشيء المفروض فيها اقتران مفض إلى إنتاج المطلوب أصلاً، واعتبره تجده كذلك. ونحن وإن قلنا إن ما أثبت لشيء أو سلب عنه بالإمكان العام أو الخاص أو الأخص أو الإطلاق العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، جائز أن يكون المسلوب عنه للمسلوب ضرورياً، فلا تستشعر أنا إذا قلنا بالضرورة كل ب ج و بالإمكان كل أ ج أو لا شيء من أ ج أنه ليس يلزم أن يكون بالضرورة لا شيء من ب لجواز أن يكون أ وإن كان ج مسلوباً عنه بالإمكان أو الإطلاق ضرورياً ج وإذ ذاك فيكون ضرورياً لـ ب لضرورة أنه ضروري لـ ج فإنه وإن كان جائزاً مع قطع النظر عن ما قيل من المقدمة الصغرى، فليس بجائز أن يكون ضرورياً لـ ج مع صدق كون ج ضرورياً لـ ب وإلا كان ضرورياً لـ ب كونه ضرورياً للضروري، ولو كان ضرورياً لـ ب لكان ج ضرورياً لبعضه الضروري لـ ب لكون ج ضرورياً لـ ب وقد كان ج لـ أ ممكناً وهو محال. وعلى هذا إن كانت المطلقة الدائمة داخلة في عموم جهة المقدمة الأخرى كالإطلاق العام أو الإمكان العام أو الخاص فلا بد من اختلاف الكيفية؛ لما بيناه في الضرورية، وإلا فلا إنتاج وإن كان المطلقة الدائمة غير داخلة في عموم جهة المقدمة الأخرى فلا إنتاج حاصل، مع اتحاد الكيفية واختلافها كما حققناه في اختلاط الضرورية.
The first art: On defining the forms of categorical syllogisms universally. And if you make it minor, the conjunction returns to the fourth figure. As for the sixth: because even if it returns to the first by conversion of one of its premises, they are both negative and there is no syllogism in the first figure from two negatives. As for the seventh: because if you convert the major, it becomes particular and there is no syllogism from two particulars; and if you convert the minor, it is not useful, as known in the third conjunction. As for the eighth: because if you convert the minor, it is not useful, as known in the fourth conjunction, and the major has no conversion because it is a possible negative. And on this, if the major were necessary and the minor possible, what is clarified by conversion and reduction to the first figure is the first and third conjunctions, by converting the major from them as before, and what is other than them is not clarified by conversion. As for the second: because if you convert the major it becomes particular; if you keep it as major, there is no production from the first figure; and if you make it minor, it returns to the fourth figure and beyond, for the known reason. As for assumption (iftirad), it clarifies only the fourth conjunction as we explained; other than that, from the necessary concomitants of the supposed thing, no conjunction leading to production of the sought conclusion is valid at all; and consider it and you will find it so. And although we say that what is affirmed of a thing or negated of it by general possibility, or specific, or most specific, or by general absolute, or specific, or most specific, it is possible that what is negated of it is necessary for the thing negated – do not think that when we say by necessity: 'Every B is J' and by possibility: 'Every A is J' or 'No A is J', that it does not follow that by necessity no B is A, because it is possible that A, even if J is negated of it by possibility or absolute, is necessary as J, and then it would be necessary for B because of the necessity that it is necessary for J. For although it is possible when disregarding what is said of the minor premise, it is not possible that it is necessary for J while it is true that J is necessary for B; otherwise it would be necessary for B, being necessary for what is necessary. And if it were necessary for B, then J would be necessary for some of its necessary things for B because J is necessary for B, whereas J was possible for A, and that is absurd. And on this, if the perpetual absolute is included in the generality of the modality of the other premise, like general absolute or general or specific possibility, then difference in quality is necessary, as we explained in the necessary; otherwise there is no production. And if the perpetual absolute is not included in the generality of the modality of the other premise, no production occurs, whether the quality is the same or different, as we verified in the mixing of the necessary.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines conditions under which categorical syllogisms reduce to the first figure and examines modal restrictions. The author begins by discussing the sixth through eighth conjunctions of a specific figure (likely the fourth figure, as indicated). For the sixth: even if it could return to the first figure by conversion of one premise, both premises become negative, and the first figure cannot produce from two negatives. For the seventh: converting the major makes it particular, and two particulars yield no conclusion; converting the minor is useless as per the third conjunction. For the eighth: converting the minor is useless per the fourth conjunction, and the major cannot be converted because it is a possible negative (affirmative possible does not convert). If the major is necessary and the minor possible, only the first and third conjunctions are clarified by conversion and reduction; others are not. Then the author addresses an issue with modality: when one premise is necessary and the other is possible or absolute, it might seem that a negative conclusion is not necessary. But the author argues that if the necessary premise is 'Every B is J' and the possible premise is 'Every A is J' or 'No A is J', it does not follow that necessarily no B is A because A could be necessary as J regardless of the modality. However, they show that if J is necessary for B, then J cannot be possible for A without contradiction. The section concludes with a condition on 'perpetual absolute' (al-mutlaq al-da'im): if it falls under the general modality of the other premise, a difference in quality (affirmative/negative) is required; otherwise no production. This is a detailed expansion of syllogistic theory, especially concerning modality, building on earlier discussions of necessary and mixed syllogisms.

Reduction to the first figure fails when conversion leads to two negative premises, two particular premises, or when a premise (possible negative) cannot convert.

The Syllogism

Modal Restrictions in Mixed Syllogisms
Author resolves modal interaction between necessary and possible premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conclusion necessarily follows when premises mix necessary (daruri) and possible (mumkin) or absolute (mutlaq) modalities, based on the conditions described.

Core Idea
Mixing necessary and possible premises restricts which conclusions are valid – a seeming counterexample is resolved by analyzing necessity composition.

The author addresses a subtle modal issue: when one premise is necessary and the other is possible or absolute, it might seem that a negative conclusion is not necessary. He argues that if the necessary premise has subject B and predicate J, and the possible premise has subject A and also J, then A cannot be both possibly J and such that J is necessary for B without contradiction. This leads to conditions on the 'perpetual absolute' – if it falls under the other premise's modality, quality must differ, otherwise no conclusion. Understanding these restrictions is vital for evaluating arguments in kalam that mix eternal and contingent statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal premises
The author considers a necessary premise 'Every B is J' (daruri) and a possible premise 'Every A is J' or 'No A is J' (mumkin).
2
Consider the apparent counterexample
One might think that if 'Every B is J' is necessary and 'No A is J' is possible, it does not necessarily follow that 'No B is A' (since A could be necessary as J in another respect).
3
Follow the author's reductio argument
He argues that if J is necessary for B, then J cannot be merely possible for A while also being necessarily true of B's necessary objects. This yields a contradiction, proving that the negative conclusion does follow necessarily.
Assume: (1) Necessarily all B are J. (2) Possibly all A are J (or possibly no A is J). If we suppose that not-necessarily no B is A, we derive a contradiction using modal closure.
4
Apply the condition for perpetual absolute
If the perpetual absolute (mutlaq da'im) falls under the other premise's modality (e.g., general absolute or general possible), then the qualities must differ; otherwise no production.
If the absolute is not included, no conclusion at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Syllogism

Form vs. Matter in Syllogistic Productivity
Objection and answer on the source of syllogistic productivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal validity and material-contextual validity in syllogistic reasoning, and explain the author's defense of the latter.

Core Idea
Syllogistic productivity depends on the truth of premises, not just the form.

This passage presents a classic objection: if a syllogism's validity comes from its form alone, it must hold in every matter. But the author's scheme admits that some conjunctions are productive only in certain matter. The author resolves this by refining the definition of qiyas: a syllogism is not just a form, but an inference where the conclusion follows necessarily from the admission of the premises for their own sakes. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange and its implications for Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's dilemma
The objector posits two horns: either productivity is from the syllogistic form itself (then it must be uniform in all matter) or from the specific matter (then all sixteen conjunctions should be productive in some matter). The objector claims neither holds, so the author's classification is inconsistent.
The 'sixteen conjunctions' refer to all possible combinations of quantified premises in the first figure.
2
Locate the author's redefinition of qiyas
The author responds by rejecting the premise that a syllogism is defined as yielding a conclusion from form alone. Instead, it is 'what requires, from the granting of categorical statements in it for their own sakes, the granting of the sought conclusion.' This shifts the criterion from pure form to the actual truth of the premises.
The phrase 'li dhatiha' (for their own sakes) emphasizes that the premises must be true and necessary, not just formally arranged.
3
Understand the subtlety of the response
The author admits that productivity varies with matter. Thus, conjunctions said to be productive in a figure are those where the conclusion follows necessarily given the premises; those not productive fail because no such necessary following occurs. The syllogism is realized only in the productive cases.
This is a material-contextual view: validity is not independent of content.
4
Apply to modal conditions
The author then clarifies that in this figure, production requires necessity (darura) or conditional (mashruta) premises. Mixed premises (necessary + possible) still yield a necessary conclusion if the necessary premise is absolute. This connects the material view to modal logic.
The discussion continues beyond the provided text, but the key principle is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ما قيل بإنتاجه من اقترانات هذا الشكل إن كانت فيه لازمة عن نفس الصورة القياسية فيلزم اطراده في جميع المواد، ولا يختلف في المطلقات الصرفة والممكنات الصرفة، ولا من خلط بعضها مع بعض كما ذكرتموه، وإن لم يكن ذلك لنفس الصورة القياسية، بل لخصوص المادة فينبغي أن لا يخرج شيء من الاقترانات الستة عشر الكائنة من المحصورات بعضها مع بعض، عن أن يكون منتجاً، وأن تكون اقترانات كل شكل ستة عشر، إذ ما من اقتران منها إلا ويصح لزوم المطلوب عنه في بعض المواد، وإن لم يصح في غيرها. قلنا ليس القياس عبارة عما يلزم المطلوب فيه عن نفس الصورة القياسية فقط ليلزم ما ذكروه، بل عبارة عما يلزم من تسليم الأقوال الجازمة فيه لذاتها تسليم المطلوب على ما سبق في تحديد القياس، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى أن الاقترانات التي قيل بكونها منتجة هذا الشكل وغيره بهذه الصفة ولا كذلك في ما قيل بعدم إنتاجه إذ هو مما يختلف لاختلاف المواد كما سبق، فيكون القياس متحققاً في ما قيل بإنتاجه دون ما لم يُقل بإنتاجه، وهو دقيق فليتأمل. وعلى ما حققناه فلا إنتاج في هذا الشكل من غير الضرورة، أو المشروطة، بالاعتبار المذكور، وما اختلط بإحداهما أو منهما، إلا أن ما تألف من الضروري المطلق صرفاً كان أو مختلطاً، فالنتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا ضرورية كما سلف. وأما الضروري المشروط فإن كان من جنس واحد فقد سبق حكمه، وإن كان مختلطاً فلا بُدّ فيه من التفصيل: فنقول: إذا قلنا كل ما هو ب دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو ج ما دام ب فإن
For it is said: What is said to be productive from the conjunctions of this figure – if the conclusion follows from the syllogistic form itself, then it must be uniform in all matter, and not differ in pure absolutes and pure possibles, nor in mixing some with each other as you have mentioned. And if it is not due to the syllogistic form itself but to the particular matter, then none of the sixteen conjunctions composed of quantified propositions with each other should be non-productive, and the conjunctions of each figure should be sixteen, since there is no conjunction among them except that the necessary following of the sought conclusion is valid in some matter, even if not valid in others. We say: The syllogism is not defined as what requires the sought conclusion solely from the syllogistic form itself, such that what they mentioned would follow; rather, it is defined as what requires, from the granting of the categorical statements in it for their own sakes, the granting of the sought conclusion, as previously in the definition of syllogism. And on this, it is not hidden that the conjunctions said to be productive in this figure and others are of this description, while those said to be non-productive are not; for it is something that differs with difference of matter as before. Thus the syllogism is realized in what is said to be productive, not in what is not said to be productive. And this is subtle, so let it be pondered. And based on what we have verified, there is no production in this figure without necessity or the conditional (mashruta) according to the mentioned consideration, and what is mixed with one or both of them. Except that what is composed of the absolute necessary, whether pure or mixed, the conclusion is only necessary as before. As for the conditional necessary, if it is of one type, its ruling has preceded; if mixed, then detail is necessary. So we say: If we say 'Everything that is B, permanently as long as the subject endures, is J as long as it is B' then...
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical exchange: an objection (naqd) and its answer (jawab), followed by further clarification. The objection argues that if syllogistic productivity is due solely to the logical form (sura qiyasiyya), then it must hold invariantly across all matter (madda). The objector notes that the author's scheme admits that certain conjunctions (of the sixteen in each figure) are productive only in some matter, not all. This suggests that productivity depends on matter, not form, contradicting the definition of a valid syllogism. The author responds by refining the definition: a syllogism is not merely what yields a conclusion from its form alone, but what, given the premises as true (taslim al-aqwal al-jazima), the conclusion necessarily follows. This necessity can depend on the specific propositions, not just the abstract schema. The author then reiterates that production in this figure requires necessity (darura) or conditional (mashruta) premises. The section ends with a beginning of an analysis of mixed conditional necessary syllogisms, leaving the discussion incomplete. This is a key methodological point: the author defends a material-contextual view of logical validity, against a purely formalist interpretation.

صورة قياسية
مادة

B) The definition of a syllogism includes that the conclusion follows from the admission of the premises for their own sake, not just the form.
C) All sixteen possible conjunctions are productive in every figure.
D) Productivity depends solely on the matter, not on the form.

Key Takeaway

Recognizing a Logical Dilemma in Dialectical Exchange
Objector's dilemma against productivity claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dilemma argument in a jadal exchange and analyze its logical structure.

Core Idea
A dilemma forces a choice between two unacceptable options; the skill is to expose hidden assumptions.

In classical Islamic dialectic (jadal/munazara), the objector often presents a dilemma to corner the claimant. This passage from al-Qazwini's work contains a classic dilemma: if productivity is from form, then it must hold for all matters; if from matter, then all combinations should be productive. The claimant must navigate this. In this lesson, we will learn how to recognize and dissect such a dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's target
What claim is being attacked? The claim that only certain combinations in each figure are productive.
Look to the beginning of the objection: 'ما قيل بإنتاجه من اقترانات هذا الشكل'.
2
Find the two horns
The objector presents two conditional statements. Locate them in the Arabic: 'إن كانت فيه لازمة عن نفس الصورة القياسية' and 'وإن لم يكن ذلك لنفس الصورة القياسية، بل لخصوص المادة'.
Each horn leads to a problematic consequence.
3
Determine the conclusion the objector wants
The objector aims to show the original claim is unsupported: either universal productivity or sixteen productive combinations per figure. Both contradict the original claim.
The consequences are absurd from the objector's perspective.
4
Analyze the logical structure
Are the two horns exhaustive and exclusive? What assumption underlies them? The objector assumes productivity depends solely on form or solely on matter, ignoring a combined view.
This reveals a false dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ما قيل بإنتاجه من اقترانات هذا الشكل إن كانت فيه لازمة عن نفس الصورة القياسية فيلزم اطراده في جميع المواد، ولا يختلف في المطلقات الصرفة والممكنات الصرفة، ولا من خلط بعضها مع بعض كما ذكرتموه، وإن لم يكن ذلك لنفس الصورة القياسية، بل لخصوص المادة فينبغي أن لا يخرج شيء من الاقترانات الستة عشر الكائنة من المحصورات بعضها مع بعض، عن أن يكون منتجاً، وأن تكون اقترانات كل شكل ستة عشر، إذ ما من اقتران منها إلا ويصح لزوم المطلوب عنه في بعض المواد، وإن لم يصح في غيرها. :قلنا ليس القياس عبارة عما يلزم المطلوب فيه عن نفس الصورة القياسية فقط ليلزم ما ذكروه، بل عبارة عما يلزم من تسليم الأقوال الجازمة فيه لذاتها تسليم المطلوب على ما سبق في تحديد القياس، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى أن الاقترانات التي قيل بكونها منتجة هذا الشكل وغيره بهذه الصفة ولا كذلك في ما قيل بعدم إنتاجه إذ هو مما يختلف لاختلاف المواد كما سبق، فيكون القياس متحققاً في ما قيل بإنتاجه دون ما لم يُقل بإنتاجه، وهو دقيق فليتأمل.
If it is said: What has been said about its productivity from the combinations of this figure, if it is necessary from the syllogistic form itself, then it must be universal across all matters, and it would not differ in pure absolutes and pure possibles, nor in mixing some with others as you have mentioned. And if it is not due to the syllogistic form itself but rather due to the specific matter, then none of the sixteen combinations arising from quantified propositions with each other should be excluded from being productive, and the combinations of each figure should be sixteen, since there is no combination among them except that the required conclusion follows from it in some matters, even if it does not follow in others. We say: The syllogism is not defined as that whose required conclusion follows from the syllogistic form itself alone, as they would require, but rather it is defined as that from the granting of decisive statements in it, due to themselves, the granting of the required conclusion follows, according to what preceded in the definition of syllogism. On this basis, it is not hidden that the combinations said to be productive in this figure and others have this quality, and likewise those said not to be productive, since it differs with differences in matter as preceded. Thus, the syllogism is realized in what is said to be productive, not in what is not said to be productive. It is precise, so let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objection followed by a refutation regarding the source of syllogistic productivity in the fourth figure. The objector argues: if productivity were solely a matter of logical form, it would apply uniformly to all subject matters (mawadd), including pure absolute and pure possible premises and their mixtures. On the other hand, if it were due to the specific matter, then all sixteen possible combinations of four quantified propositions (A, E, I, O) should be productive in at least one matter. The author responds by first clarifying the definition of qiyas: a syllogism is not merely what follows from its form, but rather that from which, once the decisive (jazima) premises are granted, the conclusion necessarily follows due to the premises themselves. This nuance integrates both form and matter. Therefore, the combinations deemed productive are those where the conclusion follows from the premises in all matters; those deemed non-productive do not universally yield a conclusion, though they might in some particular matters. The author calls this a precise point requiring careful reflection.

Key Terms
القياس
الصورة القياسية
المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two horns of the objector's dilemma in this passage?
A) Productivity due to form leads to universal validity; productivity due to matter leads to no combinations being productive.
B) Productivity due to form leads to universal validity; productivity due to matter leads to all sixteen combinations being productive.
C) Productivity due to form leads to no combinations being productive; productivity due to matter leads to universal validity.
D) Productivity due to form leads to all matters differing; productivity due to matter leads to only some combinations being productive.

Key Takeaway
The objector's dilemma is refuted by clarifying that syllogistic definition involves both form and matter, breaking the false dichotomy.

The Role of Form and Matter in Syllogistic Productivity
Author's refutation by redefining qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the definition of qiyas integrates both form and matter, and apply this to determine a syllogism's productivity.

Core Idea
A syllogism's productivity requires both correct logical form and decisive premises with appropriate matter.

The author's refutation hinges on redefining qiyas: it is not merely a formal structure but requires that the conclusion follows from the premises themselves due to their decisiveness. This means that productivity depends on both the shakl (figure) and the madda (material content). In this lesson, we will unpack this definition and its implications for determining which syllogistic combinations are productive.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of qiyas from the passage
The author defines qiyas as: 'That from which, upon granting decisive statements, the required conclusion necessarily follows due to those statements themselves.' Note the two key components: decisive statements (matter) and the following of conclusion (form).
This definition is given in response to the objection.
2
Identify the two elements: form (sura) and matter (madda)
Form is the logical structure (e.g., figure and mood); matter is the content and quality (decisiveness, universality) of the premises. In the definition, 'decisive statements' refer to the matter, and 'the required conclusion necessarily follows' depends on the form.
Both are necessary for productivity.
3
Understand the claim about productivity
The author says that combinations said to be productive have this quality (the conclusion follows from premises themselves in all matters), while those not productive lack it; the difference lies in the matter. For example, first figure moods are productive in all matters.
This explains why not all 16 combos are universally productive.
4
Apply the definition to a specific example
Take the valid first figure mood Barbara: 'Every A is B, every B is C, so every A is C.' In any matter where premises are true, the conclusion follows. This is productive because both form and matter are suitable.
If premises are not decisive (e.g., probable), the definition would not guarantee productivity.

Conditions for Production in the Fourth Figure: Necessary and Conditional Premises
Author details production conditions when mixing necessary and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal premise combinations produce a conclusion in the fourth figure and determine the modality of the conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure only yields conclusions from necessary or conditional premises, with strict modal mixing rules.

In the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms, not every modal combination produces a valid conclusion. The author establishes that only necessary (daruri) or conditional (mashruta) premises, or certain mixes of these, yield conclusions. This lesson covers the basic conditions for production and the modality of the resulting conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourth figure restriction
The author states that only necessary or conditional premises (with the specific consideration) yield a conclusion in this figure. No production occurs from other types.
The 'specific consideration' refers to the earlier discussion of the fourth figure's conditions.
2
Identify the pure necessary case
When both premises are absolute necessary (daruri mutlaq), whether pure or mixed, the conclusion is necessarily true, as previously established.
3
Recognise the mixed necessary-conditional case
If one premise is necessary and the other is conditional, the conclusion is necessary regardless of which is major or minor. The author gives an example with necessary minor and conditional major.
4
Note the conditional necessary distinction
If the conditional premise is itself necessary (conditional necessary), and it is of one type, its rule is already known. If it is mixed with other premisetypes, further detail is needed, which will be explored in later lessons.
This sets up the need for detailed analysis in the subsequent passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ما حققناه فلا إنتاج في هذا الشكل من غير الضرورة، أو المشروطة، بالاعتبار المذكور ، وما اختلط بإحداهما أو منهما، إلا أن ما تألف من الضروري المطلق صرفاً كان أو مختلطاً، فالنتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا ضرورية كما سلف. وأما الضروري المشروط فإن كان من جنس واحد فقد سبق حكمه، وإن كان مختلطاً فلا بُدّ فيه من التفصيل: فنقول: إذا قلنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") فإن كان مختلطاً مع الضروري فالنتيجة ضرورية، وسواء كانت الضرورية كبرى أو صغرى، أما إذا كانت صغرى كقولنا بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء "ب" "أ" ما دام وإلا فبعض "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ" بالإمكان العام وكان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" ما دام "أ" فبعض "ب" ليس "ج" بالإمكان العام لما سبق، وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") خلف وعليك باعتباره والضرورية كبرى. وهو عداه وإن كان المشروط مختلطاً بغير الضروري المطلق فإما أن يكون مختلطاً بما من المشروطات أو غيرها من المطلقات والممكنات، فإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط فإما أن يكون هو القسم الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع من المشروط بشرط مع الموضوع، أو بشرط المحمول أو الوقت فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الثاني من شرط الموضوع فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كان صغرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و (لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائماً، بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ") فالنتيجة (بالإطلاق لا شيء مما هو ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع) وذلك لأنه مهما كان "ج" مسلوباً عن "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع في وقت وجب أن يكون "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع مسلوباً عن "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع؛ ضرورة امتناع خلو "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع عن "ج". وبيان ذلك بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق ما ذكرناه لصدق بعض ما هو "ب" "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع دائم وكان (لا شيء مما هو الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائم بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ" فيلزم أن يكون (بعض) ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع ليس لا دائماً وكان (كل "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" دائم) وهو خلف.
And based on what we have verified, there is no production in this figure except from the necessary or the conditional, in the aforementioned consideration, and what is mixed with one of them or both, except that what is composed of the absolute necessary, whether pure or mixed, the conclusion therein is only necessary as previously. As for the conditional necessary, if it is of one type, its rule has preceded, and if it is mixed, then detail is necessary: We say: If we say "Every B that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B", then if it is mixed with the necessary, the conclusion is necessary, regardless of whether the necessary is major or minor. But if it is minor, as our saying: "Necessarily every B is J" and "Nothing that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as A", then the conclusion is necessarily "No B is A as long as" and otherwise some B is A as long as A is absolutely possible, and it was nothing of A is J as long as A, so some B is not J by absolute possibility due to what preceded, and it was necessarily every B is J as a contradiction. And it is besides that. If the conditional is mixed with other than the absolute necessary, then either it is mixed with what is from the conditionals or others from the absolutes and possibles. If it is mixed with a conditional, then either it is the second, third, or fourth division of the conditional with condition on the subject, or on the predicate, or on time. If it is mixed with the second division of the condition on the subject, then either it is major or minor. If it is minor, as our saying: "Every B that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B" and "Nothing that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject is J not permanently, but at some times of its being A", then the conclusion is absolutely "Nothing that is B permanent with the permanence of the subject is A permanent with the permanence of the subject". That is because whenever J is negated of A permanent with the permanence of the subject at some time, it is necessary that A permanent with the permanence of the subject is negated of B permanent with the permanence of the subject, due to the impossibility of B permanent with the permanence of the subject being devoid of J. Its demonstration is by contradiction: that if what we mentioned were not true, then some B that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject would be true as permanent, and it was "Nothing that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J not permanent but at some times of its being A", so it would follow that some B permanent with the permanence of the subject is not not permanent, and it was "Every B permanent with the permanence of the subject is J permanent", which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical exposition of the conditions under which the fourth figure produces conclusions. The author first reiterates that only necessary (daruri) or conditional (mashruta) premises can yield a conclusion in this figure. If the premises are pure absolute necessary or mixed (e.g., one necessary and one conditional), the conclusion is necessary. The conditional necessary (mashrut daruri) is then examined in detail. The author considers two main cases: when the conditional is mixed with an absolute necessary, and when it is mixed with other conditionals or with absolute/modal premises. For the first case, if the necessary premise is the minor, a schematic example is given using letters (B, J, A) representing terms. The conclusion is necessary with qualifications. The argument proceeds by looking at the temporal conditions. The second case is more involved: the conditional premise can be of different types (second, third, or fourth) based on whether the condition attaches to the subject, predicate, or time. The author focuses on the second type (condition on the subject) and considers when it is minor or major. For the minor case, a syllogism is constructed: all B is J as long as B; no A is J permanently, but at some times when A; therefore absolutely no B is A. The justification is a reductio ad absurdum (khalf): assuming the contrary leads to a contradiction, as shown. The author uses the impossibility of B being devoid of J to derive the logical necessity. This demonstrates the rigorous approach to modal syllogistics in the tradition.

Key Terms
الضروري المطلق
المشروطة
الخلف
الإطلاق العام

A) Conditional
B) Necessary
C) Absolute possible
D) Indeterminate

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, only necessary or conditional premises yield conclusions; pure necessary or mixed necessary-conditional premises yield necessary conclusions.

Mixing Conditionals: The Second Type Case with Proof by Contradiction
Author details production conditions when mixing necessary and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the proof by contradiction (khalf) for the conclusion when a conditional premise of the second type is the minor in the fourth figure.

Core Idea
When the conditional minor (subject condition) is mixed with a non-permanent universal major, the conclusion is an absolute universal negation, proven by contradiction.

In the detailed analysis of mixed conditionals, the author examines the case where the conditional is of the second type (condition on the subject). When this conditional is the minor premise, and the major is a proposition that denies permanence, the conclusion is an absolute universal negative. The proof uses reductio ad absurdum (khalf) to demonstrate the necessity of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor premise is: 'Every B that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B.' The major premise is: 'Nothing that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject is J permanently, but only at some times when it is A.'
This is the second type of conditional because the condition (permanence of subject) attaches to the subject term.
2
State the conclusion
The conclusion is: 'Absolutely nothing that is B permanent with the permanence of the subject is A permanent with the permanence of the subject.' This is an absolute universal negative without temporal qualification.
The phrase 'absolutely' (bi-l-itlaq) indicates no temporal condition.
3
Understand the justification
The author argues: whenever J is negated of A (at some time), then A must be negated of B at all times, because B is always J (from the minor). If some B were A at some time, then that B would have J (from minor) and also not have J (from major at that time), which is impossible.
4
Follow the khalf proof
Assume the opposite: some B that is permanent is A permanent. Then, from the major, that B would not be J permanently (since no A permanent is J permanently). But from the minor, that B is J permanently (since every B permanent is J as long as B). Contradiction. Therefore the assumption is false, and the conclusion holds.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum (khalf). The impossibility of B being devoid of J forces the negation.

Source Text

وعلى ما حققناه فلا إنتاج في هذا الشكل من غير الضرورة، أو المشروطة، بالاعتبار المذكور ، وما اختلط بإحداهما أو منهما، إلا أن ما تألف من الضروري المطلق صرفاً كان أو مختلطاً، فالنتيجة فيه لا تكون إلا ضرورية كما سلف. وأما الضروري المشروط فإن كان من جنس واحد فقد سبق حكمه، وإن كان مختلطاً فلا بُدّ فيه من التفصيل: فنقول: إذا قلنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") فإن كان مختلطاً مع الضروري فالنتيجة ضرورية، وسواء كانت الضرورية كبرى أو صغرى، أما إذا كانت صغرى كقولنا بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء "ب" "أ" ما دام وإلا فبعض "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ" بالإمكان العام وكان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" ما دام "أ" فبعض "ب" ليس "ج" بالإمكان العام لما سبق، وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") خلف وعليك باعتباره والضرورية كبرى. وهو عداه وإن كان المشروط مختلطاً بغير الضروري المطلق فإما أن يكون مختلطاً بما من المشروطات أو غيرها من المطلقات والممكنات، فإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط فإما أن يكون هو القسم الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع من المشروط بشرط مع الموضوع، أو بشرط المحمول أو الوقت فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الثاني من شرط الموضوع فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كان صغرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و (لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائماً، بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ") فالنتيجة (بالإطلاق لا شيء مما هو ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع) وذلك لأنه مهما كان "ج" مسلوباً عن "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع في وقت وجب أن يكون "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع مسلوباً عن "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع؛ ضرورة امتناع خلو "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع عن "ج". وبيان ذلك بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق ما ذكرناه لصدق بعض ما هو "ب" "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع دائم وكان (لا شيء مما هو الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائم بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ" فيلزم أن يكون (بعض) ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع ليس لا دائماً وكان (كل "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" دائم) وهو خلف.
And based on what we have verified, there is no production in this figure except from the necessary or the conditional, in the aforementioned consideration, and what is mixed with one of them or both, except that what is composed of the absolute necessary, whether pure or mixed, the conclusion therein is only necessary as previously. As for the conditional necessary, if it is of one type, its rule has preceded, and if it is mixed, then detail is necessary: We say: If we say "Every B that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B", then if it is mixed with the necessary, the conclusion is necessary, regardless of whether the necessary is major or minor. But if it is minor, as our saying: "Necessarily every B is J" and "Nothing that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as A", then the conclusion is necessarily "No B is A as long as" and otherwise some B is A as long as A is absolutely possible, and it was nothing of A is J as long as A, so some B is not J by absolute possibility due to what preceded, and it was necessarily every B is J as a contradiction. And it is besides that. If the conditional is mixed with other than the absolute necessary, then either it is mixed with what is from the conditionals or others from the absolutes and possibles. If it is mixed with a conditional, then either it is the second, third, or fourth division of the conditional with condition on the subject, or on the predicate, or on time. If it is mixed with the second division of the condition on the subject, then either it is major or minor. If it is minor, as our saying: "Every B that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J as long as B" and "Nothing that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject is J not permanently, but at some times of its being A", then the conclusion is absolutely "Nothing that is B permanent with the permanence of the subject is A permanent with the permanence of the subject". That is because whenever J is negated of A permanent with the permanence of the subject at some time, it is necessary that A permanent with the permanence of the subject is negated of B permanent with the permanence of the subject, due to the impossibility of B permanent with the permanence of the subject being devoid of J. Its demonstration is by contradiction: that if what we mentioned were not true, then some B that is A permanent with the permanence of the subject would be true as permanent, and it was "Nothing that is permanent with the permanence of the subject is J not permanent but at some times of its being A", so it would follow that some B permanent with the permanence of the subject is not not permanent, and it was "Every B permanent with the permanence of the subject is J permanent", which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical exposition of the conditions under which the fourth figure produces conclusions. The author first reiterates that only necessary (daruri) or conditional (mashruta) premises can yield a conclusion in this figure. If the premises are pure absolute necessary or mixed (e.g., one necessary and one conditional), the conclusion is necessary. The conditional necessary (mashrut daruri) is then examined in detail. The author considers two main cases: when the conditional is mixed with an absolute necessary, and when it is mixed with other conditionals or with absolute/modal premises. For the first case, if the necessary premise is the minor, a schematic example is given using letters (B, J, A) representing terms. The conclusion is necessary with qualifications. The argument proceeds by looking at the temporal conditions. The second case is more involved: the conditional premise can be of different types (second, third, or fourth) based on whether the condition attaches to the subject, predicate, or time. The author focuses on the second type (condition on the subject) and considers when it is minor or major. For the minor case, a syllogism is constructed: all B is J as long as B; no A is J permanently, but at some times when A; therefore absolutely no B is A. The justification is a reductio ad absurdum (khalf): assuming the contrary leads to a contradiction, as shown. The author uses the impossibility of B being devoid of J to derive the logical necessity. This demonstrates the rigorous approach to modal syllogistics in the tradition.

The Rule of Necessary Premises in Mixed Syllogisms
Author explains the rule for mixed syllogisms involving necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a mixed syllogism with a necessary premise produces a necessary conclusion, using an Islamic kalam example.

Core Idea
One necessary premise makes the whole conclusion necessary.

This lesson explores a key modal rule in categorical syllogisms: when one premise is necessary (ḍarūrī) and the other is not, the conclusion still emerges as necessary. The author illustrates this with a proof by contradiction, a method familiar in Islamic logic and kalām. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments that combine eternal truths with contingent facts.

Guided Path
1
State the core rule
Read the author's claim: if a syllogism mixes a necessary premise (whether major or minor) with any other modality, the conclusion is necessary. This is the rule you will apply.
The author explicitly says 'whether the necessary is major or minor'.
2
Parse the example with variables
The author gives an abstract example: 'By necessity every B is J' (necessary major) and 'Nothing that is f permanent in respect of subject is J as long as A' (non-necessary minor). The conclusion is 'Nothing that is B is A as long as A' (necessary).
The variable 'f' is corrupt, but the logic is clear: the necessary premise drives the conclusion.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
The author proves the conclusion by khalaf (reductio). Assume the opposite: some B is A. Combining with the premises leads to a contradiction, so the original conclusion must hold necessarily.
This method is common in kalām for establishing necessary truths.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Construct a mixed syllogism: Major (necessary): 'Everything temporally originated requires a creator.' Minor (assertoric): 'The world is temporally originated.' Conclusion (necessary): 'The world requires a creator.' Verify that the conclusion is necessary because the major is necessary.
This is a classic kalām argument (dalīl al-ḥudūth).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان مختلطاً مع الضروري فالنتيجة ضرورية، وسواء كانت الضرورية كبرى أو صغرى، أما إذا كانت صغرى كقولنا بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج" و(لا شيء مما هو "f" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" فالنتيجة بالضرورة لا شيء "ب" "أ" ما دام وإلا فبعض "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ" بالإمكان العام وكان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" ما دام "أ" فبعض "ب" ليس "ج" بالإمكان العام لما سبق، وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") خلف وعليك باعتباره والضرورية كبرى. وهو عداه
If it is mixed with the necessary, then the conclusion is necessary, whether the necessary is major or minor. As for if it is minor, as when we say: by necessity every B is J, and nothing that is f permanent in respect of subject is J as long as A, so the conclusion is by necessity: nothing that is B is A as long as A, otherwise some B is A as long as A with general possibility, and nothing from A is J as long as A, then some B is not J with general possibility due to what preceded, and by necessity every B is J, a contradiction. And consider it; and the necessary is major. And beyond that.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines the rule for categorical syllogisms where one premise is necessary (dɑrūrī). The author states that if a necessary premise is mixed with any other modality, the conclusion is necessary, regardless of whether the necessary premise is the major or the minor. He then gives an example when the necessary premise is the minor: 'By necessity every B is J' and 'Nothing that is f (likely a variable) permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as A.' The conclusion drawn by necessity is 'Nothing that is B is A as long as A.' The proof is by contradiction (khalaf): if the conclusion were false, then some B would be A as long as A with general possibility, but since the premises would then lead to a contradiction, the original conclusion holds. The author then notes that the same applies when the necessary premise is the major (kubrā) and indicates that further cases follow.

Mixing with the Second Division of Shart al-Mawdu'
Author details the conclusion when mixing with the second division of conditional by subject condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the conclusion in a syllogism where a conditional premise from the second division of subject condition is mixed with a categorical premise.

Core Idea
When mixing a conditional by subject condition, the conclusion's modality depends on which premise is conditional.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explores how to determine the conclusion when a categorical syllogism contains a conditional premise from the second division of shart al-mawdu'. The author walks through two cases—when the conditional is the minor premise and when it is the major—and proves the conclusion by contradiction. Understanding this mixing is essential for advanced modal logic in Islamic scholarship, particularly in debates about temporality and permanence.

Guided Path
1
Classify the conditional premise
Identify the conditional premise as belonging to the second division of subject condition. In the first case, the conditional is 'Every B is J as long as B' (universal affirmative permanent). Note its modality: it holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence.
The second division is defined by the predicate being true only while the subject exists.
2
Formalize the premises with variables
Represent the premises using Arabic letters as in the text: for the minor case, minor premise: ∀x (Bx → □_B Jx) (B is J permanently as long as B exists); major premise: ∀x (Ax → ◇¬Jx) (A is J only sometimes, not permanently). Note the negation in the major: 'No A permanent is J permanently' means every A permanent is J non-permanently.
The temporal operators are crucial: □_B means 'as long as B exists', and ◇¬J means 'not permanently J'.
3
Construct the proof by contradiction
Assume the opposite of the desired conclusion: some B is A permanently. From the minor premise, that B is J permanently. From the major premise, any A is J non-permanently, so that same B (being A) is J non-permanently. This contradicts the permanence from the minor premise, proving the conclusion 'No B is A permanently'.
The contradiction arises because the modal qualifiers are incompatible: permanent vs. not permanent.
4
Analyze the major case
When the conditional is the major premise: major: 'Every B is J non-permanently' (J at some times only); minor: 'No A is J permanently'. The same conclusion follows by a similar contradiction: if some B were A permanently, then from the major that B would be J non-permanently, but from the minor any A (including that B) is J permanently, contradiction.
Notice the roles swap but the logical structure is symmetric.
5
Generalize to other conjunctions
The author states 'consider it in the remaining conjunctions', meaning the same method applies when the conditional is mixed with other modalities (e.g., absolute, possible). The proof by contradiction remains the primary tool.
This shows the robustness of the reductio method in modal syllogistics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان المشروط مختلطاً بغير الضروري المطلق فإما أن يكون مختلطاً بما من المشروطات أو غيرها من المطلقات والممكنات، فإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط فإما أن يكون هو القسم الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع من المشروط بشرط مع الموضوع، أو بشرط المحمول أو الوقت فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الثاني من شرط الموضوع فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كان صغرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب") و (لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائماً، بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ") فالنتيجة (بالإطلاق لا شيء مما هو ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع) وذلك لأنه مهما كان "ج" مسلوباً عن "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع في وقت وجب أن يكون "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع مسلوباً عن "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع؛ ضرورة امتناع خلو "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع عن "ج". وبيان ذلك بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق ما ذكرناه لصدق بعض ما هو "ب" "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع دائم وكان (لا شيء مما هو الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائم بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ" فيلزم أن يكون (بعض) ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع ليس لا دائماً وكان (كل "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" دائم) وهو خلف. وإن كان كبرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" لا دائم، بل في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") و(لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ") فالنتيجة كنتيجة ما قبله وبيانه بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق المطلوب المذكور صدق نقيضه وهو (بعض "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع " أ " الدائم بدوام الموضوع دائم، وكان لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" فيلزمه بعض ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع ليس "ج" دائماً)، وكان كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" في وقت وهو خلف واعتبره في باقي الاقترانات.
And if the conditional is mixed with something other than absolute necessary, then either it is mixed with something from the conditionals or others from the absolutes and possibles. If it is mixed with the conditional, then either it is the second or third or fourth division of the conditional by condition of subject, or by condition of predicate or time. If it is mixed with the second division of subject condition, then either it is major or minor. If it is minor, as when we say: 'Everything that is B permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as B', and 'Nothing that is A permanent in respect of the subject is J not permanently, but at some times of its being A', then the conclusion is (absolutely) 'Nothing that is B permanent in respect of the subject is A permanent in respect of the subject'. That is because whenever J is denied of A permanent in respect of the subject at a time, it is necessary that A permanent in respect of the subject be denied of B permanent in respect of the subject, due to the necessity of impossibility of B permanent in respect of the subject being empty of J. And its proof by contradiction: if what we mentioned were not true, then some B permanent in respect of the subject would be A permanent in respect of the subject permanently, and 'Nothing that is permanent in respect of the subject is J not permanently but at some times of its being A', so it follows that some B permanent in respect of the subject is not not permanently, and 'Every B permanent in respect of the subject is J permanently', which is a contradiction. And if it is major, as when we say: 'Everything that is B permanent in respect of the subject is J not permanently, but at some times of its being B', and 'Nothing that is A permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as A', then the conclusion is like the conclusion of what preceded, and its proof by contradiction: if the aforementioned desired conclusion were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some B permanent in respect of the subject is A permanent in respect of the subject permanently', and 'Nothing that is A permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as A', so it would follow that some B permanent in respect of the subject is not J permanently, and 'Every B permanent in respect of the subject is J at a time', which is a contradiction. And consider it in the remaining conjunctions.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses mixing a categorical syllogism with a conditional premise that belongs to the second division of the conditional by subject condition (sharṭ al-mawḍū‘). The author first lays out the classification: mixing can be with the second, third, or fourth division, or with condition by predicate or time. He then focuses on the second division and considers two cases: when the conditional is the minor premise and when it is the major. In the minor case, the premise is a universal affirmative permanent ('Every B is J as long as B') and the other premise is a universal negative eternal but not permanent ('Nothing that is A is J permanently, but at some times'). The conclusion is a universal negative permanent: 'Nothing that is B is A permanently.' The proof is by contradiction: assuming the opposite (some B is A permanently) leads to a conflict with the premises. In the major case, the conditional is 'Every B is J not permanently but at some times', and the other is 'Nothing that is A is J as long as A'. The conclusion follows similarly, and the proof by contradiction is provided. The author emphasizes that the same method applies to other conjunctions.

بشرط الموضوع

Key Takeaway
When mixing with a conditional by subject condition, the conclusion is a universal negative permanent, regardless of which premise is conditional.

Mixing with Conditional by Predicate Condition
Author explains mixing with predicate condition and proves conclusions by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a mixed syllogism when one premise is a conditional by predicate condition, using proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
Mixing with predicate condition yields absolute universal negative conclusions.

In previous lessons, we examined mixing with subject condition. Now we turn to predicate condition (sharṭ al-maḥmūl), where the predicate holds only as long as the predicate itself exists. The author shows that in both subcases (conditional as minor or major), the conclusion is an absolute universal negative. The proof relies on contradiction, demonstrating the power of this method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional premise
Determine whether the conditional is by subject condition (sharṭ al-mawḍūʿ) or predicate condition (sharṭ al-maḥmūl). Here we focus on predicate condition, where the predicate is true only while the predicate itself exists.
In the passage, the phrase 'بشرط المحمول' indicates this type.
2
Distinguish minor vs major
Check whether the conditional by predicate condition appears as the minor premise or the major premise. The author treats both cases separately.
In the minor case, the conditional has the subject term 'B' as its subject; in the major case, the conditional is the major premise.
3
Apply the conclusion rule
For predicate condition, the conclusion is an absolute (muṭlaq) universal negative proposition. This is the same for both minor and major cases, though the exact form differs slightly.
Absolute here means without temporal restriction: the conclusion is simply true at the time of utterance.
4
Verify by contradiction
Assume the opposite of the conclusion (i.e., that some B is A or that some B is not A, depending on the case). Combine with the other premise to derive a contradiction with the conditional premise.
The author shows that if the desired conclusion were false, its contradictory would lead to a contradiction with the conditional premise, proving the conclusion must hold.
