وإن كان اختلاطه مع القسم الثالث والرابع فالنتيجة على ما تقدم من الاختلاط بالقسم الثاني، وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى، والبيان على ما تقدم. وإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط بشرط المحمول فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى فإن كان صغرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج" ما دام "ب")، و(لا شيء مما هو "أ" "ج" ما دام "ج") والنتيجة (لا شيء من "ب" ما دام "ب" "أ" بالإطلاق). وبيانه بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق ما ذكرناه وإلا لصدق نقيضه، وهو دائماً بعض "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "أ") وكان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" ما دام "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "ج"، وكان (كل "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج") وهو خلف. وإن كان كبرى كقولنا : (كل "ب" "ج" ما دام "ج") و (لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" ) ، والنتيجة بالإطلاق العام لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ")، والبيان بالخلف هو أنه إن لم يصدق المطلوب المذكور صدق نقيضه، وهو (بعض "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ" دائماً) وكان (لا شيء من "أ" "ج" دائم) فيلزمه (ليس كل "ب" "ج" دائماً) وهو خلف.
And if its mixture is with the third and fourth divisions, the conclusion is according to what preceded from the mixture with the second division, whether it is major or minor, and the proof is according to what preceded. And if it is mixed with the conditional by condition of predicate, then either it is major or minor. If it is minor, as when we say: 'Everything that is B permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as B', and 'Nothing that is A is J as long as J', then the conclusion is (absolutely) 'Nothing from B as long as B is A'. And its proof by contradiction: if what we mentioned were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely 'Always some B permanent in respect of the subject is A', and 'Nothing from A is J as long as J', so 'Nothing from B permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as J', and 'Every B permanent in respect of the subject is J', which is a contradiction. And if it is major, as when we say: 'Every B is J as long as J', and 'Nothing that is A permanent in respect of the subject is J as long as A', then the conclusion is with absolute generality: 'Nothing from B is A as long as A'. And the proof by contradiction is: if the aforementioned desired conclusion were not true, its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some B is A as long as A permanently', and 'Nothing from A is J permanently', so it would follow that 'Not every B is J permanently', which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed syllogisms with conditional premises. First, the author states that mixing with the third and fourth divisions of the subject condition (sharṭ al-mawḍū‘) follows the same rule as the second division, so no new proof is needed. Then he turns to mixing with the condition of predicate (sharṭ al-mahmul), which is a different type. He again examines two subcases: when the conditional by predicate condition is the minor premise and when it is the major. In the minor case, the conditional is 'Every B is J as long as B' and the other premise is 'Nothing that is A is J as long as J'. The conclusion is an absolute universal negative: 'Nothing that is B is A as long as B'. The proof by contradiction shows that if some B were A, then with the second premise (nothing from A is J as long as J) it would imply that some B is not J as long as J, contradicting the conditional premise that every B is J as long as B. In the major case, the conditional is 'Every B is J as long as J' and the other is 'Nothing that is A is J as long as A'. The conclusion is an absolute universal negative: 'Nothing from B is A as long as A'. The proof again uses contradiction. The key point is that different conditional types yield different modal conclusions.

الإطلاق

Handling Time Condition in Mixed Syllogisms via Analogy
Rule for mixing with time condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a time condition (sharṭ al-waqt) in a mixed syllogism and derive the conclusion by analogically applying the rules from the second, third, and fourth divisions.

Core Idea
Time condition follows earlier divisions; no new rule—just analogical reasoning.

In this very short passage, the author completes the discussion of mixed syllogisms involving conditional premises. He addresses the special case where the condition is a time condition (sharṭ al-waqt)—whether the time is specified or unspecified. Rather than providing a new rule, he directs you to apply the same reasoning used in the second, third, and fourth divisions of the earlier classification. This teaches a powerful method: when you encounter a new conditional type, you do not start from scratch; you use analogical extension (i‘tibār) from known cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the time condition
The passage deals with mixing a categorical premise with a conditional premise that has a time condition (sharṭ al-waqt). This means the predicate holds only at a specific or unspecified time. Recognize such conditionals in your analysis.
Examples: 'If it is Fajr time, then the prayer is valid.' The time is specified; 'If it is daytime, then fasting is valid.' The time is unspecified but still limited.
2
Recall the rules from earlier divisions
The author mentions that previously, for mixing with the second, third, and fourth divisions of the subject condition, specific conclusion patterns were established. You need to remember those patterns. They serve as the 'original case' (asl) for analogy.
The book earlier covered these divisions in detail. Review them if necessary.
3
Apply analogical reasoning (i'tibār)
Since the time condition case shares the same structure as the earlier divisions (it is a type of conditional mixing), you apply the same conclusion rules. This is i‘tibār: extending the known ruling to a new but similar case.
The 'illa (effective cause) is that both are conditional restrictions on the predicate.
4
Derive the conclusion
Following the pattern of the earlier divisions, determine the conclusion of the mixed syllogism. For example, if the second division required affirming the consequent under certain conditions, then do the same here.
The passage does not repeat the rules, so you must reason analogically.

And if its mixture is with the conditional by condition of time, whether the time is determined or undetermined, the conclusion is according to what preceded in its mixture with the second, third, and fourth divisions, so you must consider it.

The reader is directed to apply analogical reasoning (i‘tibār) to determine the exact conclusion. This shows that the earlier detailed cases serve as a model for other similar conditional types.

Reduction Proof for Mixed Syllogisms with Temporal Modalities
Author provides conclusion rule and khalf proof for a mixed syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reduction proof (al-khalf) for a mixed categorical syllogism involving the 'perpetual with permanence of subject' modality and identify the contradiction that validates the conclusion.

Core Idea
Reduction to absurdity (الخلف) assumes the opposite conclusion and derives a contradiction, proving the original.

This passage illustrates how a logician proves a syllogistic conclusion using the method of khalf (reduction to absurdity). The author states that the conclusion of this mixed syllogism is the same as in a previously discussed case, then provides the proof: assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion, and from the premises derive an impossible pair of statements about the terms B and C—specifically, that some B is not C perpetually and every B is C at some time. This contradiction shows that the original conclusion must be true. Mastering khalf is essential for validating complex syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion
From the context, the conclusion being proved is the one that follows from the prior divisions of propositions with temporal modalities. The passage does not state it explicitly, but it is the conclusion of the mixed syllogism.
The exact wording is corrupted; focus on the logical structure.
2
Assume the contradictory
To prove by khalf, assume the negation of the desired conclusion. The passage says: 'if the aforementioned sought conclusion is not true, its contradictory is true'.
This assumed contradictory is taken as a temporary premise.
3
Derive a contradiction using the premises
From the assumed contradictory together with the original premises, deduce two statements that cannot both be true. In the passage, the derived contradiction is: 'some B that is perpetual with permanence of subject is not C perpetually' and 'every B that is perpetual with permanence of subject is C at some time'.
The terms A, B, C are placeholders with temporal modalities.
4
Recognize the impossibility
The two derived statements directly contradict each other—hence the assumption leads to an absurdity (khalf). Therefore, the original assumption (the negation of the conclusion) is false, so the conclusion is true.
This is the essence of reduction to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنتيجة كنتيجة ما قبله وبيانه بالخلف، وهو أنه لو لم يصدق المطلوب المذكور صدق نقيضه وهو (بعض "ب" الدائم بدوام الموضوع "أ" الدائم بدوام الموضوع دائم، وكان لا شيء مما هو "أ" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "أ" فيلزمه بعض ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع ليس "ج" دائماً)، وكان كل ما هو "ب" دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" في وقت وهو خلف واعتبره في باقي الاقترانات.
The conclusion is like the conclusion of what preceded it, and its demonstration is by reduction to absurdity, which is that if the aforementioned sought (conclusion) is not true, its contradictory is true, which is (some B that is perpetual with permanence of subject is A perpetual with permanence of subject? perpetual, and there is nothing that is A perpetual with permanence of subject is C as long as it is A, so it follows that some B that is perpetual with permanence of subject is not C perpetually), and (every B that is perpetual with permanence of subject is C) at some time, which is a contradiction. And consider it in the remaining conjunctions.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage provides the rule for the conclusion of a mixed syllogism involving the third and fourth divisions of propositions. The author states that the conclusion is the same as in the previously discussed case. The proof uses reduction to absurdity (الخلف). The author assumes the contradictory of the desired conclusion and derives a contradiction involving the terms B and C. Specifically, from the assumption that the conclusion is false, the author derives that there is a time when some B is not C and also that every B is C, which is absurd. This validates the original conclusion. The student should note that this method is common in logic for establishing syllogistic moods.

Conclusion Rule for Mixed Syllogisms with Third and Fourth Divisions
Author states that the conclusion for mixing with third and fourth divisions follows the same rule as mixing with the second division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for mixing with the third and fourth divisions of propositions in a syllogism, given prior knowledge of the rule for the second division.

Core Idea
The rule for mixed premises in divisions three and four is identical to division two's rule.

In this brief passage, the author condenses a key principle: when a syllogism involves premises from the third or fourth divisions (types of propositions classified in Arabic logic), the rule for deriving the conclusion is exactly the same as the rule already established for mixing with the second division. This holds whether the mixed premise is the major or minor. The author assumes you already know the second division rule from earlier in the text. Your task is to recall that rule and apply it consistently.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule for mixing with the second division
From your previous lesson, what is the rule for concluding when one premise is from the second division? If you haven't studied that, review now. The second division rule is the baseline.
2
Identify the mixed premise division
In your current syllogism, determine whether the premise from the third/fourth division is the major or minor. The passage says it doesn't matter which one is mixed.
3
Apply the same rule regardless of position
Use the same conclusion-formation rule you recalled in step 1, regardless of whether the mixed premise is major or minor.
4
Verify the demonstration pattern
The passage notes that the demonstration (al-bayan) is the same as previously given. So any proof you would offer for the second division rule applies here too.

And if its mixing is with the third and fourth divisions, then the conclusion is according to what has preceded of mixing with the second division, whether it is major or minor, and the demonstration is according to what has preceded.

Mixing Absolute General Premises in Syllogisms
Author explains mixing categorical syllogisms with absolute and modal premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion when mixing absolute general premises with per se premises in categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality follows the weaker premise, not the stronger.

In categorical logic, premises can be absolute (temporal) or modal (necessary, possible). When mixing an absolute general premise (true at some time) with a per se premise (true always), the conclusion inherits the absolute general modality. This lesson unpacks the rules for such mixed syllogisms using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and modalities
Read the source passage and note the two premises: one is absolute general (e.g., 'Every B is C by absolute general'), the other is per se (e.g., 'No A is C per se'). Determine which is minor and which is major.
2
Apply the reductio proof
If the absolute general is minor, assume the opposite of the conclusion (some B is A per se). Combine with the per se major (No A is C per se) to derive a contradiction with the absolute general minor.
This proves the conclusion must be absolute general.
3
Generalize the rule
When the absolute general is major, the proof is analogous. For special absolute, treat as per conditional mixing rules.
The conclusion always takes the weaker modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان مختلطاً بغير المشروطات فإما بالمطلقات أو الموجهات، فإن كان مختلطاً بالمطلقات فإما بالمطلقة العامة أو الخاصة، فإن كانت مطلقة عامة فإما أن تكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كانت العامة صغرى كقولنا: (كل ب ج بالإطلاق العام) و(لا شيء مما أ دائم بدوام الموضوع ج ما دام أ) ونتيجته (لا شيء من ب أ الدائم بدوام الموضوع بالإطلاق العام) وذلك لأن ج مسلوب عن أ دائماً، وهو ثابت لـ ب بالإطلاق العام، فلا يكون ب أ بالإطلاق العام. وبيانه بالخلف، وهو أنه إن لم يصدق (لا شيء من ب أ بالإطلاق العام) صدق (بعض ب أ دائم) وكان (لا شيء من أ ج دائم) فيلزم (ليس كل ب ج دائماً) وكان (كل ب ج بالإطلاق العام) وهو خلف. وإن كانت المطلقة كبرى كقولنا: (كل ب ما دام ب فهو ج) و(لا شيء من ج بالإطلاق) فالنتيجة (لا شيء مما هو ب دائم بدوام الموضوع أ) كنتيجة ما قبله.

وإن كان مختلطاً بالمطلقة الخاصة فالحكم على ما تقدم في اختلاطه بالقسم الثاني والثالث من المشروطات بشرط الموضوع.

وإن كان مختلطاً بالموجهات فإن كان ما اختلط معه ضرورياً فقد سبق حكمه. وإن كان ممكناً فإن كان ممكناً بالمعنى العام فالنتيجة ممكنة عامة، وسواء كانت الممكنة صغرى أو كبرى، وكذلك الحكم في ما إذا كانت الممكنة بالمعنى الخاص، وعليك بالاعتبار.
And if it is mixed with other than conditionals, then either with absolutes or modals. If mixed with absolutes, then either with the general absolute or the special absolute. If it is general absolute, then either it is major or minor. If the general absolute is minor, as in our statement: 'Every B is C by absolute general', and 'No A is C per se while A exists', then its conclusion is 'No B is A per se by absolute general', because C is negated from A always, and it is affirmed of B by absolute general, so B is not A by absolute general. Its explanation by reductio: if 'No B is A by absolute general' is not true, then 'Some B is A always' is true, and 'No A is C always' holds, so it follows that 'Not every B is C always' while 'Every B is C by absolute general', which is a contradiction. And if the absolute general is major, as in our statement: 'Every B as long as it is B is C', and 'No C by absolute', then the conclusion is 'Nothing that is B is A always' similar to the previous conclusion.

And if it is mixed with the special absolute, the ruling is as previously mentioned in its mixing with the second and third divisions of conditionals with a condition of subject.

And if it is mixed with modals, then if what is mixed with it is necessary, its ruling has preceded. And if it is possible, then if it is possible in the general sense, the conclusion is generally possible, whether the possible is minor or major. Likewise the ruling when the possible is in the specific sense, and you rely on consideration.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains how categorical syllogisms work when mixed with absolute (non-modal) and modal premises, specifically focusing on the general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma) and special absolute (mutlaqa khassa), as well as modal propositions like necessary (daruriyya) and possible (mumkina). The author first considers the case where the general absolute is the minor premise. Using the example where 'Every B is C by absolute general' is the minor, and 'No A is C per se (daim bi dawam al-mawdu') while A exists' is the major, the conclusion is 'No B is A per se by absolute general'. The reasoning is that C is always negated from A (since the major says no A is C per se), but C is affirmed of B by absolute general (meaning at some time, not necessarily always). Therefore, B cannot be A per se, because if it were, then B would be C (by the minor) and not C (via A) leading to contradiction. The author then provides a reductio proof: assume the conclusion false, i.e., 'Some B is A always'; then since 'No A is C always', it follows that 'Some B is not C always', but the minor says 'Every B is C by absolute general', which is a contradiction.

When the general absolute is the major premise, as in 'Every B as long as it is B is C' (a conditional major) and 'No C by absolute general' (the minor), the conclusion is 'No B is A always', similar to the previous case. The proof is analogous.

Next, mixing with special absolute (mutlaqa khassa) is briefly stated to follow the same rules as mixing with the second and third divisions of conditionals under the condition of the subject.

Finally, mixing with modal propositions: if the modal premise is necessary (daruriyya), the ruling is already known (presumably that the conclusion is necessary). If it is possible (mumkina), then if it is possible in the general sense (al-ma'na al-'amm), the conclusion is generally possible, regardless of whether the possible is minor or major. The same applies if the possible is in the specific sense (al-ma'na al-khass). The author ends with 'and you rely on consideration', indicating that the student should work out further details.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة
المطلقة الخاصة
الموجهات
الضرورية
الممكنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In mixing syllogisms, if the minor premise is 'every intoxicant is forbidden by absolute general' and the major is 'no nabidh is intoxicant per se', what is the modal status of the conclusion?
A) Necessary (per se)
B) Absolute general
C) Absolute special
D) Impossible

Key Takeaway
Mixing an absolute general premise with a per se premise yields an absolute general conclusion, not a necessary one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Divisions of Necessary Conditionals
Author enumerates divisions of necessary conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four divisions of necessary conditionals (al-ḍarūriyya al-mashrūṭa) and distinguish them based on their conditions.

Core Idea
The four divisions of conditionals determine their behavior in syllogisms.

In Islamic logic, necessary conditionals are classified into divisions based on the conditions attached to the subject or predicate. This lesson introduces those divisions as a foundation for understanding when mixing them yields valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Locate Condition Types
Scan the Arabic for terms like 'bi-sharṭ al-mawḍūʿ' (condition of subject), 'bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl' (condition of predicate), and 'bi-sharṭ al-waqt' (condition of time). Each indicates a different division.
The second division is necessary with condition of subject; the third is 'not always with permanence of subject'; the fourth is another type.
2
Identify the Four Divisions
The passage mentions 'second division', 'third division', 'fourth division', and also condition of predicate and time. Understand each: second: necessary as long as subject exists; third: not always true with subject permanence; fourth: likely similar to 'condition of predicate'? Actually, the fourth is mashruta bi-shart al-haml?, but the author treats condition of predicate separately.
The taxonomy may vary; focus on the distinctions used in the argument.
3
Define Necessary Conditional
A necessary conditional with condition of subject (e.g., 'Every B is C necessarily as long as B exists') holds only while the subject exists. Contrast with the third division which is 'not always with permanence of subject'.
This is crucial for mixing rules.
4
Map Divisions to Examples
The text gives an example for the second and third mixture: 'Everything that is B always with permanence of subject is C not always' (second) and 'Nothing that is A not always with permanence of subject is C while A exists' (third). Construct similar examples for other divisions.
Practice generating your own examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الضرورية المشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع من القسم الثاني، فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الثالث فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى كقولنا: (كل ما هو ب دائم بدوام الموضوع ج لا دائم، ولا شيء مما هو أ لا دائم بدوام الموضوع ج ما دام أ) فالنتيجة (لا شيء مما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع أ الذي ليس دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإلا فالصادق دائماً بعض ب المفروض أ المفروض، وكان كل ب ج دائم) وهو خلف. وعلى هذا تكون النتيجة، وإن كان كبرى.

وإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الرابع، أو بشرط المحمول، أو بشرط الوقت، أو بالمطلق العام أو الخاص، أو الأخص، أو بالممكن العام أو الخاص، أو الأخص فلا إنتاج لجواز اختلاف سلب ج وإيجابه للأكبر والأصغر، وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى.

وإن كان من القسم الثالث، فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الرابع فإن كان صغرى كقولنا: (كل ب لا دائم بدوام الموضوع ج ما دام ب) و (لا شيء مما هو لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع ج في بعض أوقات كونه أ) فالنتيجة (لا شيء مما هو ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع أ الذي ليس دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإلا فالصادق دائماً بعض ب المفروض أ المفروض)، وكان لا شيء من لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع ج لا دائماً بل في بعض أوقات كونه أ، فيلزم ليس كل ب المفروض ج لا دائماً، وكان كل ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع ج دائماً بدوام ب، وهو خلف، وعلى هذا فلا تخفى النتيجة إذا كان كبرى.

وإن كان مختلطاً بشرط مع المحمول فلا إنتاج، وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى؛ لأن الحد الأوسط في المشروط بشرط المحمول وإن كان ضروري السلب أو الإيجاب فليس إلا في حالة سلبه وإيجابه، لأنه ضروري السلب أو الإيجاب مطلقاً، بل جاز أن يكون ثابتاً وجاز أن يكون مسلوباً؛ ولهذا فإنا لو قلنا (كل أبيض فهو مفرق للبصر ما دام أبيض) و (لا شيء من الألوان مفرق للبصر حالة كونه غير مفرق للبصر) فإنه لا ينتج (لا شيء من الأبيض لون).

وإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط بشرط وقت فسواء كان معيناً أو غير معين فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كان صغرى كقولنا (كل ما هو ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فإنه ج ما دام ب) و (لا شيء من أ ب في وقت) فإنه ينتج (لا شيء مما هو ب ما دام ب لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع أ) واعتبره بالخلف.
And if the necessary conditional with a condition of the subject from the second division, then if it is mixed with the third division, either it is major or minor, as in our statement: 'Everything that is B always with permanence of subject is C not always', and 'Nothing that is A not always with permanence of subject is C while A exists' — the conclusion is 'Nothing that is B always with permanence of subject is A which is not always with permanence of subject', otherwise 'Some B assumed is A assumed' would always be true, and 'Every B is C always' — which is a contradiction. And thus the conclusion, even if it is major.

And if it is mixed with the fourth division, or with condition of predicate, or condition of time, or with the general or specific absolute, or the more specific, or with the general or specific possible, or the more specific, then there is no production due to the possibility of difference in the negation and affirmation of C for the major and minor, whether it is major or minor.

And if it is from the third division, and it is mixed with the fourth division, then if it is minor as in our statement: 'Every B not always with permanence of subject is C as long as B', and 'Nothing that is not always with permanence of subject is C at some time of its being A' — the conclusion is 'Nothing that is B not always with permanence of subject is A which is not always with permanence of subject', otherwise 'Some B assumed is A assumed' would always be true, and 'Nothing that is not always with permanence of subject is C not always' but rather at some times of its being A, so it follows that 'Not every B assumed is C not always', and 'Every B not always with permanence of subject is C always with permanence of B' — which is a contradiction. And thus the conclusion is not hidden if it is major.

And if it is mixed with condition of predicate, then there is no production, whether it is major or minor, because the middle term in the conditional with condition of predicate, even if it is necessary in negation or affirmation, it is only in the state of its negation and affirmation, because it is absolutely necessary in negation or affirmation, rather it may be affirmed or negated. For this, if we say 'Every white is color-dispersing as long as it is white' and 'None of the colors is color-dispersing in the state of it not being color-dispersing', then it does not produce 'None of the white is colored'.

And if it is mixed with the conditional with condition of time, whether it is determinate or indeterminate, then either it is major or minor. If it is minor as in our statement: 'Everything that is B not always with permanence of subject is C as long as B', and 'Nothing of A is B in some time' — then it produces 'Nothing that is B as long as B is not always with permanence of subject A' — and consider it by reductio.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage deals with mixing necessary conditional premises (al-daruriyya al-mashruta) with other types of conditionals and absolutes. The author first considers the case where the necessary conditional is from the second division (mashruta bi-shart al-mawdu') and mixed with the third division (la da'im bi dawam al-mawdu'). An example is given: the minor premise 'Everything that is B always (with permanence of subject) is C not always' and the major 'Nothing that is A not always is C while A exists'. The conclusion is 'Nothing that is B always is A which is not always' — proven by reductio. The same applies if the necessary conditional is major.

Next, when mixing with the fourth division (mashruta bi-shart al-haml?), or with condition of predicate (bi-shart al-mahmul), condition of time (bi-shart al-waqt), or various absolutes (general, specific, more specific) or possibles (general, specific, more specific), no valid conclusion can be drawn because the middle term's negation or affirmation can vary for the larger and smaller terms.

Then the author discusses a case where the necessary conditional is from the third division (la da'im) and mixed with the fourth division. An example with minor from third and major from fourth is given, again with a reductio proof. The conclusion is again 'Nothing that is B not always is A which is not always'.

Next, mixing with condition of predicate (bi-shart al-mahmul) yields no conclusion, because the middle term in such a conditional is necessary only in the state of its own predicate being affirmed or negated, not absolutely. An illustrative example: 'Every white is color-dispersing as long as it is white' (conditional) and 'None of the colors is color-dispersing in the state of it not being color-dispersing' (another conditional) does not imply 'None of the white is colored'. The author explains that the middle term 'color-dispersing' is not fixed across both premises.

Finally, mixing with condition of time (bi-shart al-waqt), whether determinate or indeterminate, can yield a conclusion. The minor premise is from the third division (la da'im) and the major is a temporal conditional: 'Nothing of A is B in some time'. The conclusion is 'Nothing that is B as long as B is A not always' — to be verified by reductio.

Throughout, the author uses the technical apparatus of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) within the framework of mixed categoricals, showing when a valid conclusion can be drawn.

Key Takeaway
The divisions of necessary conditionals are distinguished by the type of condition they impose, and this classification is essential for evaluating syllogistic validity.

Mixing Necessary Conditionals: Valid or Not?
Author examines each mixture type and outcome.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules to determine whether mixing two necessary conditionals yields a valid conclusion or 'la intaj'.

Core Idea
Mixing conditionals from different divisions often yields no conclusion due to middle-term instability.

Now that we know the divisions, we need to know which mixtures produce valid conclusions. The author examines each combination, showing that some produce conclusions via reductio while others result in 'la intaj' (no production). This lesson will teach you to evaluate such mixtures systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Divisions
Given a mixture, first determine the divisions of the two premises. The passage covers mixtures like 2nd with 3rd, 2nd with 4th, 3rd with 4th, and with condition of predicate or time.
Use the key terms from the previous lesson to identify the divisions.
2
Check for Condition of Predicate
If either premise has a condition on the predicate (bi-shart al-mahmul), the passage says no conclusion follows. Example: 'Every white is color-dispersing as long as it is white' (condition on predicate? Actually, condition on subject? Wait, the example has 'as long as it is white' which is condition on subject. The passage's example for condition of predicate is: 'None of the colors is color-dispersing in the state of it not being color-dispersing' — that is a condition on the predicate 'color-dispersing'. So if either premise has such a condition, no conclusion.
The middle term's truth value can vary.
3
Apply the Reductio Test
For mixtures that can produce conclusions (2nd+3rd, 3rd+4th, etc.), the author uses a reductio argument: assume the opposite of the desired conclusion leads to a contradiction. Trace one such reductio in the text.
Understanding the reductio method helps verify the conclusion.
4
Test with an Islamic Example
Take a fiqh example: 'Every sale is valid as long as both parties consent' (2nd division) and 'No sale under duress is valid' (assume it's 3rd division because duress may be temporary). Does a conclusion follow? According to the text, yes, with a reductio.
Practice building the reductio.

Rules for Mixed Modal Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains validity conditions for mixed modal categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a categorical syllogism with premises from different modal categories yields a valid conclusion, using the classifications given in the text.

Core Idea
Not every modal mix yields a conclusion; validity depends on specific modalities and their interaction.

You have already learned about categorical syllogisms where both premises share the same modal category. But what happens when one premise is necessary and the other is possible, or when one has a temporal condition? The author of this text provides detailed rules for such mixed syllogisms. This lesson will guide you through his analysis, focusing on the key categories and their outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal categories involved
The author refers to divisions (e.g., third, fourth), condition of predicate, condition of time, absolute, possible, etc. Determine which categories are combined in a given syllogism.
The categories are defined earlier in the text; familiarity with them is assumed.
2
Apply the general rule for non-productive mixes
Mixing with the fourth division, condition of predicate, condition of time, absolute, or possible (except when specified) often yields no conclusion. This is because the middle term may be affirmed and negated differently for the two extremes.
The author states 'no production' for such mixtures, with exceptions later.
3
Analyze a productive mix using reductio
When the minor premise is from the third division and the major from the fourth, a conclusion can be derived. The author provides an example with variables (أ, ب, ج) and shows that assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction (khalaf).
Work through the variable example step by step to see how the conclusion follows.
4
Examine the condition of predicate case
In mixing with 'condition of the predicate', no conclusion follows. The middle term is necessary only in a qualified state (e.g., 'as long as it is white'), not absolutely, so it cannot connect the extremes.
The concrete example (white, vision, colors) illustrates why the syllogism fails.

وإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الرابع، أو بشرط المحمول، أو بشرط الوقت، أو بالمطلق العام أو الخاص، أو الأخص، أو بالممكن العام أو الخاص، أو الأخص فلا إنتاج لجواز اختلاف سلب "ج" وإيجابه للأكبر والأصغر، وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى. وإن كان من القسم الثالث، فإن كان مختلطاً بالقسم الرابع فإن كان صغرى كقولنا: (كل "ب" لا دائم بدوام الموضوع "ج" ما دام "ب") و (لا شيء مما هو لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "ج" في بعض أوقات كونه "أ") فالنتيجة (لا شيء مما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "أ" الذي ليس دائماً بدوام الموضوع، وإلا فالصادق دائماً بعض "ب" المفروض "أ" المفروض)، وكان لا شيء من لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "ج" لا دائماً) بل في بعض أوقات كونه "أ") فيلزم ليس كل "ب" المفروض "ج" لا دائماً) وكان ( كل "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "ج" دائماً بدوام "ب") وهو خلف، وعلى هذا فلا تخفي النتيجة إذا كان كبرى. وإن كان مختلطاً بشرط مع المحمول فلا إنتاج، وسواء كان كبرى أو صغرى؛ لأن الحد الأوسط في المشروط بشرط المحمول وإن كان ضروري السلب أو الإيجاب فليس إلا في حالة سلبه وإيجابه، لأنه ضروري السلب أو الإيجاب مطلقاً، بل جاز أن يكون ثابتاً وجاز أن يكون مسلوباً؛ ولهذا فإنا لو قلنا كل أبيض فهو مفرق للبصر ما دام أبيض) و (لا شيء من الألوان مفرق للبصر حالة كونه غير مفرق للبصر) فإنه لا ينتج (لا شيء من الأبيض لون). وإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط بشرط وقت فسواء كان معيناً أو غير معين فإما أن يكون كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كان صغرى كقولنا كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فإنه "ج" ما دام "ب") و (لا شيء من "أ" "ب" في وقت) فإنه ينتج (لا شيء مما هو "ب" ما دام "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "أ") واعتبره بالخلف. وإن كان كبرى أنتج (لا شيء من "ب" الذي ليس دائماً بدوام الموضوع "أ" لا دائماً) واعتبره بالخلف. وإن كان مختلطاً بالضروري المطلق أو الخاص أو الممكن العام أو الخاص أو الأخص فالنتائج على ما تقدم في القسم الأول. وإن كان القسم الرابع، فإن كان مختلطاً بالمشروط بشرط وقت فلا إنتاج؛ لجواز اختلاف وقت سلب الأوسط وإيجابه للطرفين كما سبق. وكذا إن كان مختلطاً بالمطلق العام أو الخاص أو الأخص واعتبره.
And if it is mixed with the fourth division, or with the condition of the predicate, or with the condition of time, or with the absolute general or specific or more specific, or with the possible general or specific or more specific, then there is no production because of the possibility of difference in the negation and affirmation of 'ج' for the major and minor, whether it is major or minor. And if it is from the third division, then if it is mixed with the fourth division, then if it is minor, as in our saying: (Every 'ب' is not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'ج' as long as 'ب') and (Nothing that is not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'ج' at some times of its being 'أ'), then the conclusion is (Nothing that is 'ب' is not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'أ' which is not permanent in the permanence of the subject; otherwise, the true always some 'ب' assumed 'أ' assumed), and there was nothing of not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'ج' not permanent) but at some times of its being 'أ'), so it follows that not every 'ب' assumed 'ج' not permanent) and there was (Every 'ب' not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'ج' permanent in the permanence of 'ب'), which is a contradiction, and on this the conclusion is not hidden if it is major. And if it is mixed with the condition with the predicate, then there is no production, whether it is major or minor; because the middle term in the conditioned with the condition of the predicate, even if it is necessary in negation or affirmation, is only in the state of its negation and affirmation, because it is necessary in negation or affirmation absolutely, rather it is possible to be established and possible to be negated; and for this reason if we say: Every white is that which separates vision as long as it is white) and (Nothing from colors separates vision in the state of its being not separating vision), then it does not produce (Nothing from white is color). And if it is mixed with the conditioned with the condition of time, whether definite or indefinite, then either it is major or minor, so if it is minor, as in our saying: Everything that is 'ب' is not permanent in the permanence of the subject so it is 'ج' as long as 'ب') and (Nothing from 'أ' is 'ب' at a time), then it produces (Nothing from what is 'ب' as long as 'ب' is not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'أ') and consider it by reduction to contradiction. And if it is major, it produces (Nothing from 'ب' which is not permanent in the permanence of the subject 'أ' is not permanent) and consider it by reduction to contradiction. And if it is mixed with the absolute necessary or specific or the possible general or specific or more specific, then the conclusions are as previously in the first division. And if it is the fourth division, then if it is mixed with the conditioned with the condition of time, there is no production; because of the possibility of difference in the time of negation of the middle and its affirmation for the two extremes as before. And likewise if it is mixed with the absolute general or specific or more specific, and consider it.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical discussion of the validity conditions for categorical syllogisms when the premises are mixed from different modal categories. The author has previously divided modal propositions into distinct categories (e.g., necessary, absolute, possible, conditioned by predicate, conditioned by time, etc.). Here, he examines what happens when a premise from one category is combined with a premise from another category.

The passage first states a general rule: mixing with the fourth division (which likely refers to a specific category, perhaps the 'conditioned by time' or 'absolute' as per previous classification) or with certain other modal types yields no conclusion, because the affirmation or negation of the middle term may relate differently to the major and minor terms.

He then takes the third division and considers its mixture with the fourth. He gives an example in the first figure where the mixed premise is minor, and shows through a reductio ad absurdum (خلف) that the conclusion can be derived. He then states the case when the mixed premise is major, leaving the conclusion to be analogously derived.

Next, he discusses mixing with 'condition with the predicate' (مشروط بشرط المحمول). He explains that no conclusion follows because the middle term, although necessary in its attribution, is not necessary absolutely but only in a qualified state. He illustrates with a concrete example: 'Every white is that which separates vision as long as it is white' and 'Nothing from colors is that which separates vision in the state of its being not separating vision' — the syllogism does not yield 'Nothing from white is color', because the middle term (separating vision) is not coextensive with the terms.

Then he treats mixing with the conditioned by time (مشروط بشرط وقت). If the minor premise is from this category, a conclusion can be derived (as shown by an example with variables), and similarly if the major is conditioned by time. He again suggests using reduction to contradiction to verify.

Finally, he returns to other combinations: mixing with absolute necessary, specific, possible categories yields conclusions according to rules already given for the first division. For the fourth division mixed with conditioned by time or absolute categories, no conclusion follows due to potential variation in time.

The passage is highly compressed and assumes familiarity with the author's classification of modal propositions. The logical variables (أ, ب, ج) are used as placeholders for terms. The overall point is that modal mixing requires careful attention to the specific modalities to determine whether a valid syllogism results.

The Third Figure: Conditions for Validity
Definition and conditions of the third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a third-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the two conditions for validity and explain why violations fail.

Core Idea
The third figure's conclusion is always particular; its validity requires an affirmative minor and at least one universal premise.

In this passage, the author defines the third figure of categorical syllogism and lays out two essential conditions for it to yield a valid conclusion. Understanding these conditions is crucial because third-figure syllogisms appear in many theological and juristic arguments, and misidentifying their validity can lead to faulty reasoning. We will break down the definition, the conditions, and the author's proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of the third figure
The middle term is the subject of both extremes, meaning the minor and major terms are both predicates of the middle. This structure forces the conclusion to be particular.
Contrast with first figure where middle is subject of major and predicate of minor.
2
Note the conclusion's nature
The author states the conclusion is always particular (جزئية) even if premises are universal. This is invariant for the third figure.
This is because the minor term is a predicate in the minor premise, so it can only be quantified particularly in the conclusion.
3
State the two conditions for productivity
The author gives two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, (2) at least one premise must be universal. Valid moods require an affirmative minor (I or A) and at least one universal (A or E).
For example, Darapti (AAI) and Datisi (AII) are valid.
4
Analyze the counterexamples
The author shows that a negative minor (E) fails regardless of the major's quality, and two particular premises fail because the middle may not be shared. Examine his examples to see the logical failure.
The example 'No human is a horse; Every human is rational' improperly yields 'Some horse is rational'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الفصل الخامس في تحقيق الشكل الثالث وقرائنه ما الحد الأوسط فيه موضوع للطرفين - أعني الأكبر والأصغر – ونتيجته لا تكون إلا جزئية، وإن كانت مقدماته كلية، وإذ ذاك كان متأخراً عن الثاني كما سبق، وأما وجه تقدمه على الرابع فما به تقدم الشكل الثاني عليه، وله في إنتاجه شرطان إيجاب صغراه، وكليّة إحدى مقدمتيه فلا قياس فيه عما صغراه سالبة، إلا أن تكون في قوة الموجبة، ولا عن جزئيتين. أما الأول فلأنه إذا كان الحد الأصغر مسلوباً عن الأوسط فما يحكم به على الأوسط، إما أن يكون بإيجاب أو سلب، فإن كان الأول فليس يلزم أن يكون واجباً للأصغر، وإلا كان قولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان فرس) و(كل إنسان ناطق ناتجاً بعض الفرس ناطق وهو محال، ولا مسلوباً عنه وإلا كان هذا الاقتران إذا بدلت الناطق فيه بالحيوان ناتجاً بعض الفرس ليس بحيوان وهو محال. وأما إذا كان ذلك بالسلب فليس يلزم أيضاً انتاج، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب شيئين عن شيء أن تكون النسبة بين الشيئين بالسلب وإلا كانت النسبة بين الإنسان والناطق بالسلب ؛ لضرورة سلبهما عن الحجر، ولا بالإيجاب وإلا كانت النسبة بين الإنسان والفرس بالإيجاب ضرورة سلبهما عن الحمار، بل إن كان الأكبر له نسبة إلى الأوسط في مثل هذه الاقترانات فليس لنفس الصورة، بل لخصوص المادة. وأما الثاني، فلأنه إذا كان الاقتران من جزئيتين فالحد الأوسط غير متيقن الاشتراك بين المقدمتين، بل من الجائز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالحد الأصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالحد الأكبر، ولا قياس في الاقترانات بغير حدٍ ،متوسط، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة وإحدى المقدمتين كليّة، فإن جزأي المطلوب يلتقيان لا محالة.
And it is the fifth chapter: On the investigation of the third figure and its conditions. What is the middle term in it? It is the subject of the two extremes – I mean the major and the minor – and its conclusion is only particular, even if its premises are universal. And then it is posterior to the second figure, as previously. As for the manner of its priority over the fourth, it is by what the second figure is prior over it. And it has for its production two conditions: the affirmation of its minor premise, and the universality of one of its two premises. Thus there is no syllogism in it from what has its minor premise negative, unless it is in the force of an affirmative, nor from two particulars. As for the first, because if the minor term is negated of the middle, then whatever is predicated of the middle, either by affirmation or negation. If it is the first, then it is not necessary that it be necessary for the minor, otherwise our statement: 'No human is a horse' and 'Every human is rational' would produce 'Some horse is rational,' which is impossible; and not negated of it, otherwise this combination if we substitute 'rational' with 'animal' would produce 'Some horse is not an animal,' which is impossible. And if it is by negation, then production is also not necessary, for it is not necessary from the negation of two things from a thing that the relation between the two things is by negation, otherwise the relation between human and rational would be by negation, due to the necessity of their being negated from stone; nor by affirmation, otherwise the relation between human and horse would be by affirmation due to the necessity of their being negated from donkey. Rather, if the major has a relation to the middle in such combinations, it is not due to the form itself but due to the particular matter. As for the second, because if the combination is from two particulars, then the middle term is not certainly shared between the two premises; rather it is possible that the part judged by the minor term is not the part judged by the major term, and there is no syllogism in combinations without a middle term. And this is contrary to when the minor is affirmative and one of the premises is universal, for the two parts of the sought conclusion meet without fail.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the third figure of categorical syllogism and its conditions for productivity. The author begins by defining the third figure: the middle term is the subject of both the major and minor terms. Its conclusion is always particular, even if both premises are universal. The third figure is posterior to the second (i.e., less perfect) but prior to the fourth, by the same criterion that makes the second prior to the fourth. The author then states two conditions for a valid syllogism in this figure: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) one of the two premises must be universal. Consequently, syllogisms with a negative minor premise are invalid unless the negative is equivalent to an affirmative (e.g., obversion), and syllogisms with two particular premises are invalid. The author explains the first condition: if the minor term is negated of the middle, then whatever is said about the middle—whether affirmative or negative—does not necessarily apply to the minor. If we assume an affirmative major, the example 'No human is a horse; Every human is rational' would yield 'Some horse is rational,' which is false. If we switch the major to negative, substituting 'rational' with 'animal', we get 'Some horse is not an animal,' also false. If the major is negative, still no necessary conclusion: from the fact that two terms (like human and rational) are both negated of a third (stone), we cannot infer either a negative or affirmative relation between them. Thus, any valid conclusion from a negative minor is accidental to the matter, not due to the form. The author then explains the second condition: with two particular premises, the middle term may refer to different parts in each premise, so there is no guarantee of a common middle linking the two extremes. This contrasts with the case where the minor is affirmative and one premise is universal, ensuring that the two parts of the conclusion meet necessarily.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الحد الأوسط
إنتاج
قوة الموجبة

Key Takeaway
A valid third-figure syllogism requires an affirmative minor premise and at least one universal premise; otherwise, no necessary conclusion follows.

The Third Figure: Definition and Productivity Conditions
Defines the third figure and states its two productivity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the third figure and apply its two productivity conditions to determine whether a given triple of propositions forms a valid third-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
The third figure always gives a particular conclusion, and needs an affirmative minor and one universal premise.

Now that you have mastered the first two figures, you encounter the third figure, which has unique constraints. In this passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, the author defines the third figure and explains its two essential conditions. Understanding these conditions will allow you to distinguish valid from invalid syllogisms in this figure, a skill directly applicable to arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
Notice that the author states: the middle term is the subject of both extremes (major and minor). This is the structural feature that makes it the third figure. Contrast this with the first figure (middle is subject of one, predicate of other) and second figure (middle is predicate of both).
2
Recognize the conclusion restriction
The author asserts that the conclusion is necessarily particular, even if both premises are universal. This is a key difference from the first figure, which can yield universal conclusions. Try to see why: because the middle term is never distributed as predicate in both premises, the major term is not fully connected to the minor.
This follows from the condition of distribution in categorical syllogisms.
3
Understand condition 1: minor must be affirmative
The author proves this by counterexample. If the minor is negative, say 'No human is horse', and the major is 'Every human is rational', then no valid conclusion follows. He shows that attempting to conclude either 'Some horse is rational' or 'Some horse is not rational' leads to false or unwarranted conclusions. This demonstrates the necessity of an affirmative minor.
The proof uses two examples: with 'rational' and 'animal' as the major term.
4
Understand condition 2: one premise must be universal
If both premises are particular, the middle term may not be shared between the two parts of the major and minor terms. The author explains that there is no guarantee that the 'some' in the minor refers to the same part as the 'some' in the major. Hence no connection is established, and no valid syllogism results.
For example, 'Some A is B' and 'Some A is C' do not allow any conclusion about B and C.
5
Apply the conditions to the 16 combinatorial moods
The author then counts that from 16 possible combinations of quantified premises (A, E, I, O for each premise), the two conditions eliminate 10, leaving 6 valid moods. The affirmative minor condition removes 8 (where minor is negative), and the universal premise condition removes 2 (both particular). Practice deriving these 6 moods yourself as an exercise.
The six moods can be pure or mixed with modalities, similar to the previous figures.

وهو الفصل الخامس في تحقيق الشكل الثالث وقرائنه ما الحد الأوسط فيه موضوع للطرفين - أعني الأكبر والأصغر – ونتيجته لا تكون إلا جزئية، وإن كانت مقدماته كلية، وإذ ذاك كان متأخراً عن الثاني كما سبق، وأما وجه تقدمه على الرابع فما به تقدم الشكل الثاني عليه، وله في إنتاجه شرطان إيجاب صغراه، وكليّة إحدى مقدمتيه فلا قياس فيه عما صغراه سالبة، إلا أن تكون في قوة الموجبة، ولا عن جزئيتين. أما الأول فلأنه إذا كان الحد الأصغر مسلوباً عن الأوسط فما يحكم به على الأوسط، إما أن يكون بإيجاب أو سلب، فإن كان الأول فليس يلزم أن يكون واجباً للأصغر، وإلا كان قولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان فرس) و(كل إنسان ناطق ناتجاً بعض الفرس ناطق وهو محال، ولا مسلوباً عنه وإلا كان هذا الاقتران إذا بدلت الناطق فيه بالحيوان ناتجاً بعض الفرس ليس بحيوان وهو محال. وأما إذا كان ذلك بالسلب فليس يلزم أيضاً انتاج، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب شيئين عن شيء أن تكون النسبة بين الشيئين بالسلب وإلا كانت النسبة بين الإنسان والناطق بالسلب؛ لضرورة سلبهما عن الحجر، ولا بالإيجاب وإلا كانت النسبة بين الإنسان والفرس بالإيجاب ضرورة سلبهما عن الحمار، بل إن كان الأكبر له نسبة إلى الأوسط في مثل هذه الاقترانات فليس لنفس الصورة، بل لخصوص المادة. وأما الثاني، فلأنه إذا كان الاقتران من جزئيتين فالحد الأوسط غير متيقن الاشتراك بين المقدمتين، بل من الجائز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالحد الأصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالحد الأكبر، ولا قياس في الاقترانات بغير حدٍ متوسط، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة وإحدى المقدمتين كليّة، فإن جزأي المطلوب يلتقيان لا محالة. الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية وقرائنه باعتبار تركب المحصورات بعضها مع بعض ستة عشر كما مضى، غير أن اشتراط إيجاب صغراه أسقط منها ثمانية وهي ما الصغرى سالبة، وقد عرف أن السالبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، وأن كل واحدة منهما قد يمكن أن تجعل لها أربع كبريات من المحصورات واشتراط كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها اثنتين، أحدهما الكائن من جزئيتين موجبتين، والآخر من جزئيتين والكبرى سالبة فعادت قرائنه المنتجة إلى ستة. وهي إما أن يكون صرفه من جنس واحد من المطلقات أو الموجهات أو مختلطة، كما سلف في قرائن الشكلين السابقين
This is the fifth chapter on the investigation of the third figure and its moods. The middle term in it is the subject of both extremes – namely the major and minor – and its conclusion is only particular, even if its premises are universal. And thus it is posterior to the second as previously mentioned. As for the reason for its priority over the fourth, it is that by which the second figure is prior to it. It has two conditions for its productivity: the affirmation of its minor premise and the universality of one of its two premises. So there is no syllogism in it from a negative minor, unless it is in force of an affirmative, nor from two particular [premises]. As for the first: because if the minor term is denied of the middle, then what is predicated of the middle is either by affirmation or negation. If the first, then it does not necessarily hold true for the minor; otherwise our statement 'No human is a horse' and 'Every human is rational' would produce 'Some horse is rational', which is impossible; nor is it denied of it, otherwise this conjunction, if 'rational' is replaced by 'animal', would produce 'Some horse is not an animal', which is impossible. And if it is by negation, then it also does not necessarily produce, because it does not follow from the negation of two things from a thing that the relation between the two things is negative; otherwise the relation between human and rational would be negative due to their necessary negation from stone; nor positive, otherwise the relation between human and horse would be positive due to their necessary negation from donkey. Rather, if the major has a relation to the middle in such conjunctions, it is not due to the form itself but due to the particular matter. As for the second: because if the conjunction is of two particulars, the middle term is not certain to be shared between the two premises; rather it is possible that the part judged by the minor term is not the part judged by the major term, and there is no syllogism in conjunctions without a middle term. This is contrary to the case where the minor is affirmative and one of the premises is universal, for then the two parts of the desired [conclusion] inevitably meet. The first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. Its moods, considering the composition of quantified [propositions] with each other, are sixteen as previously mentioned. However, the condition of an affirmative minor eliminates eight of them, those where the minor is negative. It is known that the negative is either universal or particular, and that each of them can have four major premises from the quantified [propositions]. And the condition of universality of one of its two premises eliminates two of them: one being the [conjunction] of two particular affirmatives, and the other being [the conjunction] of two particulars with the major negative. So its productive moods return to six. They are either pure from one kind of absolute or modal or mixed, as previously mentioned in the moods of the two preceding figures.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism and its essential conditions. The author begins by stating the defining characteristic of the third figure: the middle term is the subject of both the major and minor terms. Consequently, the conclusion is always particular, never universal, even if both premises are universal. This distinguishes it from the second figure (where the middle term is predicate of both) and the fourth figure (where the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor). The author then lists two necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in the third figure: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, and (2) at least one premise must be universal. He explains why a negative minor fails: using the example 'No human is a horse' (minor) and 'Every human is rational' (major), a conclusion 'Some horse is rational' would be false; similarly with 'animal' instead of 'rational'. This shows that when the minor term is denied of the middle, no necessary relation follows between the major and minor terms. He also explains why two particular premises fail: the middle term may not be distributed, so the two parts of the conclusion may not be connected. For example, if we have 'Some human is rational' and 'Some human is mortal', we cannot conclude anything about the relation between rational and mortal. The author then transitions to counting the possible moods from the 16 combinations of quantified propositions, applying the two conditions to reduce them to six valid moods. These six can be further categorized by modality (absolute, modal, or mixed) similar to previous figures.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
صغرا
إيجاب
جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because the major premise could be affirmative.
B) No, because the minor must be affirmative.
C) Yes, if the major is also negative.
D) No, because both premises must be universal.

Key Takeaway
The third figure is defined by the middle term as subject of both extremes, and its two conditions (affirmative minor and one universal premise) are necessary for any valid syllogism.

First Two Valid Moods of the Third Figure
Author presents the first two moods of the third figure with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two valid moods of the third figure (Darapti and Disamis) and verify their validity using conversion and contradiction proofs.

Core Idea
Third figure yields particular conclusions from universal premises, proven by conversion or contradiction.

In this passage, the author introduces the first two moods of the third figure, both involving affirmative propositions. You will learn how to derive a particular conclusion from two universal premises (Darapti) and from a particular and universal premise (Disamis). The proofs demonstrate logical necessity using conversion and reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and mood
The third figure has the middle term as the subject in both premises. Here, B is the middle term. The first mood has two universal affirmatives; the second has a particular minor and universal major.
2
State premises and conclusion for each mood
First mood: 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A' give 'Some C is A' (Darapti). Second mood: 'Some B is C' and 'Every B is A' give 'Some C is A' (Disamis).
3
Proof by contradiction (khalf)
Assume the opposite of the conclusion (e.g., 'No C is A') and derive a contradiction with the premises. For the first mood, if no C is A, then since every B is C, no B is A, contradicting 'Every B is A'.
4
Proof by conversion (aks)
Convert the minor premise 'Every B is C' to 'Some C is B'. Then with 'Every B is A', we get a first-figure syllogism (Darii) yielding 'Some C is A'.
5
Special reduction for the second mood
Assume a specific subset D of B that is C. Then from 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C', together with 'Every B is A', we derive 'Some C is A' via the first mood of the third figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنفرض الكلام أولاً في بيان المطلقات منها: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين ونتيجته جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ") والنتيجة ليس كل "ج" "أ") لجواز أن يكون "ج" أعم من "ب" والحكم إذ ذاك بـ "أ" على كل "ب" لا يوجب أن يكون حكماً به على كل "ج" بل على بعضه، بل النتيجة بعض "ج " "أ") وإلا فدائماً (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") وكان (كل ( "ج") فدائماً (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وكان (بالإطلاق كل "ب" "أ") وهو خلف. ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول ناتجاً (بعض "ج " "أ") وهو عين المطلوب. الاقتران الثاني من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية ونتيجته جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (بعض) "ب" "ب") و(كل "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "ج" "أ") والبيان لذلك كبيان الاقتران الذي قبله من العكس والخلف، ولأنه إذا فرض بعض "ب" الذي هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" لزمه (كل "د" "ب") و(كل "د" "ج") فإذا جعل (كل "د" "ب") صغرى لكبرى أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول (كل "د" "أ")، فإذا جعل هذا اللازم كبرى للازم الآخر أنتج من الاقتران الأول من هذا الشكل عين المطلوب.
Let us first discuss the absolute [moods] among them: The first conjunction: two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is a particular affirmative, like our saying: 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A', and the conclusion is 'Not every C is A' because C may be more general than B, and the judgment of A for every B does not necessitate that it be a judgment for every C, but rather for some of it. Rather, the conclusion is 'Some C is A'. Otherwise, it would always be 'No C is A', and if every C is... [incomplete] then always 'No B is A', and it would be 'Absolutely every B is A', which is a contradiction. Also, if you convert the minor, the conjunction returns to the third conjunction of the first figure, producing 'Some C is A', which is exactly the desired. The second conjunction: two affirmatives, with the minor particular, and its conclusion is a particular affirmative, like our saying: 'Some B is C' and 'Every B is A', so 'Some C is A'. The explanation for this is like the explanation of the previous conjunction by conversion and contradiction. Because if you assume that the part of B which is C is a specific thing, let it be D, then it follows that 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C'. Then if you take 'Every D is B' as a minor for a major, it produces from the first conjunction of the first figure 'Every D is A'. Then if you make this consequent a major for the other consequent, it produces from the first conjunction of this [third] figure exactly the desired.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the first two valid moods of the third figure using absolute (non-modal) propositions, with proofs. The first mood consists of two universal affirmative premises: 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A', yielding the particular affirmative conclusion 'Some C is A'. The author provides two proofs: (1) by contradiction: if the conclusion were 'No C is A', then, since every B is C, it would follow that no B is A, contradicting the premise 'Every B is A'. (2) By conversion: converting the minor 'Every B is C' to 'Some C is B' yields a first-figure syllogism (third mood) with the conclusion 'Some C is A'. The second mood has a particular minor and universal major: 'Some B is C' and 'Every B is A', giving 'Some C is A'. The proof mirrors the first, using conversion and contradiction. Alternatively, the author introduces a method by supposing the part of B that is C is a specific entity D. Then 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C' are true. From 'Every D is B' and 'Every B is A' we get 'Every D is A' (first figure, first mood). Then using 'Every D is C' and 'Every D is A' in the third figure's first mood, we get 'Some C is A'. The use of the letter D as a placeholder for a specific subset is a neat way to reduce the second mood to the first. In Islamic logical tradition, these moods are essential for arguments where a common attribute links two categories. For example, the first mood can represent: 'Every human is rational' and 'Every human is a living being', so 'Some living being is rational' – a valid particular conclusion.

The Syllogism

From Sixteen to Six: The Productive Conjunctions of Categorical Syllogisms
Author counts sixteen possible conjunctions and reduces them to six productive ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six productive conjunctions of a categorical syllogism and explain why the conditions of affirmative minor and universal premise eliminate ten of the sixteen possible premise pairs.

Core Idea
Out of sixteen premise pairs, only six produce a valid syllogistic conclusion.

In this passage, the author systematically reduces the number of possible premise combinations in a categorical syllogism from sixteen to six. By applying two key conditions—that the minor premise must be affirmative and that at least one premise must be universal—he shows which conjunctions are productive (produce a conclusion) and which are not. This logical housekeeping is essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where qiyas (analogical reasoning) depends on sound syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting total of 16 premise pairs
The author begins with the sixteen possible conjunctions of quantified propositions (A, E, I, O) taken two at a time. In Islamic logic, this enumeration is the basis for checking validity in categorical syllogisms.
The number 16 comes from 4 types of minor × 4 types of major (4×4=16).
2
Apply the condition of an affirmative minor premise
The author states that if the minor premise is negative (E or O), the syllogism is unproductive. This eliminates half of the sixteen: those with minor E or O. For example, 'No intoxicant is wine' (E) as minor cannot support a valid conclusion because it does not affirm the minor term's membership.
This condition reflects that in a valid syllogism, the minor term must be included in the middle term via affirmation.
3
Apply the condition of at least one universal premise
Even if the minor is affirmative, if both premises are particular (I or O), no necessary conclusion follows. The author mentions two such pairs: two particular affirmatives (I+I) and two particulars with a negative major (I+O). These two are eliminated, bringing the total eliminated to ten.
This condition ensures the syllogism has enough scope to relate the terms.
4
Confirm the remaining six productive conjunctions
After elimination, six premise pairs remain. These are the only valid starting points for a categorical syllogism. For example, the pair 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (A major) and 'Nabidh is an intoxicant' (I minor) is productive because it satisfies both conditions (minor affirmative, major universal).
The six conjunctions correspond to the valid moods of the first two figures, as noted in the text.
5
Classify the six as uniform or mixed, absolute or modal
The author notes that these six can be composed of absolute (unmodalized) propositions or modal ones, and can be uniform (same type) or mixed. He promises to first discuss the absolute ones, which are the simplest and most commonly used in fiqh and kalam.
This classification prepares for detailed study of each productive conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. وقرائنه باعتبار تركب المحصورات بعضها مع بعض ستة عشر كما مضى، غير أن اشتراط إيجاب صغراه أسقط منها ثمانية وهي ما الصغرى سالبة، وقد عرف أن السالبة إما كليّة وإما جزئية، وأن كل واحدة منهما قد يمكن أن تجعل لها أربع كبريات من المحصورات. واشتراط كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها اثنتين، أحدهما الكائن من جزئيتين موجبتين، والآخر من جزئيتين والكبرى سالبة فعادت قرائنه المنتجة إلى ستة. وهي إما أن يكون صرفه من جنس واحد من المطلقات أو الموجهات أو مختلطة، كما سلف في قرائن الشكلين السابقين، ولنفرض الكلام أولاً في بيان المطلقات منها.
The first art: on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. Its conjunctions, considering the composition of quantified propositions with each other, are sixteen as has passed, except that the condition of affirmative minor drops eight from them, which are those with a negative minor, and it is known that the negative is either universal or particular, and that each of them can have four majors from among the quantified propositions. And the condition of universality of one of its premises drops two from them, one of which is from two particular affirmatives, and the other from two particulars with a negative major; so its productive conjunctions return to six. And they are either uniform from one kind of absolutes or modals or mixed, as has preceded in the conjunctions of the two previous figures, and we will first assume speech in explaining the absolutes from them.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage opens the first art on defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. The author recaps that the number of possible conjunctions of quantified propositions is sixteen, as previously established. However, imposing the condition that the minor premise must be affirmative eliminates eight (those with a negative minor). The negative minor can be universal or particular, each combinable with four possible majors. Then the condition that at least one premise be universal removes two more: one from two particular affirmatives and one from two particulars with a negative major. This leaves six productive conjunctions. These six can be homogeneous (all absolute or all modal) or mixed. The author then states he will first discuss the absolute ones. This systematic enumeration provides the foundation for the detailed analysis of each conjunction that follows.

First Conjunction: Two Universal Affirmatives
Rule for the first productive conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first productive conjunction and determine why its conclusion must be particular, given two universal affirmative premises.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives never yield a universal conclusion in this figure.

In this passage, the author presents the first productive conjunction of the third figure. It consists of two universal affirmative premises, but its conclusion is not universal as you might expect — it is particular. Let's understand why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Find the two universal affirmatives: 'Every B is C' and 'Every B is A'. The conclusion is 'Some C is A'.
2
Understand why the conclusion is particular
The author says it is because C may be more general than B. If all B are C, but C includes things that are not B, then the judgment about B (being A) does not necessarily apply to all C.
3
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Let B = 'intoxicant', C = 'forbidden thing', A = 'impure'. Every intoxicant is forbidden, every intoxicant is impure, but some forbidden things are not impure (like dead meat). So the conclusion 'some forbidden thing is impure' holds.
The middle term here is B; it is the subject in both premises, which is a sign of the third figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين ونتيجته جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ") والنتيجة ليس كل "ج" "أ") لجواز أن يكون "ج" أعم من "ب" والحكم إذ ذاك بـ "أ" على كل "ب" لا يوجب أن يكون حكماً به على كل "ج" بل على بعضه، بل النتيجة بعض "ج" "أ") وإلا فدائماً (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") وكان (كل "ج") فدائماً (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وكان (بالإطلاق كل "ب" "أ") وهو خلف. ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول ناتجاً (بعض "ج" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, its conclusion is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Every B is A), and the conclusion is not every C is A because it is possible that C is more general than B, and the judgment then by A on every B does not necessitate that it is a judgment by it on every C but on some of it, rather the conclusion is some C is A. Otherwise, always (No C is A) and (Every C) then always (No B is A) and (Absolutely every B is A) which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, the conjunction returns to the third conjunction of the first figure producing (Some C is A) which is exactly what is required.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the first productive conjunction (اقتران) in the third figure? It consists of two universal affirmative premises, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The author gives the standard example: Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A. He explains why the conclusion cannot be universal: if C is broader than B (e.g., B=human, C=animal), then even if all B are A, it does not follow that all C are A. The proof proceeds by contradiction: assume the opposite (no C is A, i.e., universal negative), then since every B is C, we would have every B is A (contradicting the given). Alternatively, by converting the minor premise (from 'every B is C' to 'some C is B') then using it as a minor with the original major in the first figure, we obtain the desired particular affirmative. This illustrates both a reductio ad absurdum (خلف) and a conversion (عكس) method.

عكس
خلف

Key Takeaway

Proving the First Conjunction: Reductio and Conversion
Proofs (reductio and conversion) for the first conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct both the reductio ad absurdum and the conversion proof for the first productive conjunction.

Core Idea
Two logical tools — reductio and conversion — confirm the particular conclusion.

The author does not just state the rule; he proves it using two methods. First, by contradiction: assume the opposite conclusion and derive a contradiction. Second, by converting the minor premise and reconfiguring the syllogism into the first figure. Let's walk through each proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio proof (خلف)
Assume not (Some C is A), i.e., no C is A. Since every B is C, then no B is A. But we have every B is A. Contradiction. Therefore, the original conclusion (some C is A) must hold.
2
Walk through the conversion proof (عكس)
Convert the minor premise 'every B is C' to 'some C is B' (permissible by accidental conversion from A to I). Then use this as minor with the major 'every B is A' in the first figure: Every B is A, some C is B, therefore some C is A.
3
Apply the conversion proof to our Islamic example
Convert 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' to 'Some forbidden thing is intoxicant'. Then with major 'Every intoxicant is impure', we get 'Some forbidden thing is impure'.
The conversion proof shows how a third figure syllogism can be reduced to the first figure, which is the paradigmatic valid form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين ونتيجته جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ") والنتيجة ليس كل "ج" "أ") لجواز أن يكون "ج" أعم من "ب" والحكم إذ ذاك بـ "أ" على كل "ب" لا يوجب أن يكون حكماً به على كل "ج" بل على بعضه، بل النتيجة بعض "ج" "أ") وإلا فدائماً (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") وكان (كل "ج") فدائماً (لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وكان (بالإطلاق كل "ب" "أ") وهو خلف. ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول ناتجاً (بعض "ج" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, its conclusion is particular affirmative, like our saying: (Every B is C) and (Every B is A), and the conclusion is not every C is A because it is possible that C is more general than B, and the judgment then by A on every B does not necessitate that it is a judgment by it on every C but on some of it, rather the conclusion is some C is A. Otherwise, always (No C is A) and (Every C) then always (No B is A) and (Absolutely every B is A) which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, the conjunction returns to the third conjunction of the first figure producing (Some C is A) which is exactly what is required.

The author gives the standard example: Every B is C and every B is A, so some C is A. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
