Fourth Figure: First Two Moods with Proof by Contradiction
Author defines two moods of the fourth figure with proofs by contradiction and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the two valid moods of the fourth figure (universal major negative, and particular major negative) and prove their validity using proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields particular negative conclusions when the major is negative — proven by contradiction and conversion.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogisms has several moods. The author presents two: one with two universal premises and a negative major, and another obtained by converting the minor premise to a particular negative major. Both yield a particular negative conclusion. He proves each by contradiction, assuming the opposite conclusion and deriving a contradiction with the given premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood structure
The first mood has two universal premises: the minor is affirmative, the major is negative. Example: Every B is C, No B is A. The conclusion is particular negative: Some C is not A.
The major term is A, minor term is C, middle term is B.
2
Apply proof by contradiction
Assume the opposite of the conclusion: Every C is A. Combine with Every B is C (minor) to get Every B is A by Barbara (first figure). This contradicts the given major: No B is A. Hence the original conclusion must be true.
The contradiction shows the assumption is false, so the conclusion cannot be universal; thus particular negative is correct.
3
Convert the minor premise for the second mood
The second mood uses the original minor (Every B is C) and a particular negative major (Some B is not A). The conclusion is also particular negative: Some C is not A. Proof: assume Every C is A, then Every B is A contradicts Some B is not A.
This mood is akin to the fifth conjunction mentioned in the text after conversion.
4
Verify the conclusion type
In both moods, the conclusion cannot be universal because the minor term (C) may be more general than the middle term (B). The author explicitly notes this as the reason for particularity.
This reflects the rule that from a universal negative major, the conclusion must be particular.

This passage defines two moods of the fourth figure (al-iqtiran al-rabi') in categorical syllogisms. The first mood has two universal premises with a negative major, yielding a particular negative conclusion. The author explains that the conclusion cannot be universal because the minor term might be more general than the middle term. He gives an example: Every B is C, No B is A, therefore Some C is not A. He then proves the validity by contradiction: assume the opposite (Every C is A), then combine with Every B is C to get Every B is A, which contradicts No B is A. He then shows another mood by converting the minor premise: if you take the minor as universal affirmative and major as particular negative (Some B is not A), then the conclusion is Some C is not A, again proved by contradiction. This demonstrates the variety of valid moods in the fourth figure.

The Fifth Conjunction: Validity by Contradiction and Conversion
Author defines the fifth conjunction mood and proves its validity by contradiction and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the fifth conjunction mood and demonstrate its validity using contradiction and conversion.

Core Idea
Particular negative conclusions can be deduced from a universal negative major and particular affirmative minor.

In this lesson, we explore the fifth conjunction of the fourth figure. We learn how to derive a particular negative conclusion from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major. The author provides two elegant proofs: by contradiction and by conversion, which we will analyse step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Recognize that the minor is particular affirmative (Some B is C) and the major is universal negative (No B is A).
2
State the conclusion
The conclusion is particular negative: Some C is not A.
3
Proof by contradiction
Assume the opposite of the conclusion (Every C is A). Combine with the minor (Some B is C) to get Some B is A, which contradicts the major (No B is A). Thus the original conclusion must hold.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Proof by conversion
Convert the minor (Some B is C) to Some C is B. Now the major (No B is A) and the converted minor form a first-figure syllogism (Celarent) that directly yields Some C is not A.
Conversion of a particular affirmative yields a particular affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة ونتيجته جزئية سالبة كقولنا: (بعض "ب" "ج") و(لا شيء من "ب" "أ") فـ (بعض "ج" ليس "أ") وإلا فـ (دائماً كل "ج" "أ") وكان (بعض "ب" "ج") فـ (دائماً بعض "ب" "أ") وكان (بالإطلاق لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وهو خلف، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب. وإن بينت بالافتراض فرضت البعض من "ب" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزم (كل "د" "ب") و(كل "د" "ج") فـ (كل "د" "ب") إذا جعل صغرى للكبرى أنتج من الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول (لا شيء من "د" "أ") فإذا جعل هذا اللازم كبرى للازم الآخر وهو (كل "د" "ج") أنتج من الاقتران الرابع من هذا الشكل جزئية سالبة عين المطلوب، ولأنك في الكل إذا حكمت بـ "ج" على "ب" فإما على بعضه أو كله، فإن كان على بعضه فبعض "ج" هو بعض "ب" فما حكم به على كل "ب" سلباً كان أو إيجاباً يدخل فيه بعض "ج" الذي هو بعض "ب" وإن كان على كل "ب" فالحكم بـ "أ" على بعض "ب" أو كل "ب" إيجاباً أو سلباً يدخل أيضاً فيه بعض "ج" ضرورة أنه لا "ب" إلا وهو "ج" وعلى هذه البيانات فالنتيجة تكون بالإطلاق الخاص إن كانت القرينة من المطلقات الخاصة، وبالجملة فالنتيجة تابعة للقرينة الصرفة في الإطلاق والجهة بأي اعتبار كان.
The fifth conjunction from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, its conclusion is particular negative, as our saying: (Some B is C) and (No B is A), so (Some C is not A). Otherwise (always every C is A) and (some B is C) so (always some B is A) but (absolutely no B is A) which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, the conjunction returns to the fourth conjunction of the first figure producing the same sought. And if it is proven by assumption, you assume the part of B which is not A as a specific thing, let it be D. Then it follows that (Every D is B) and (Every D is C). Then (Every D is B) if made minor to the major, produces from the second conjunction of the first figure (No D is A). Then if you make this necessary major to the other necessary, which is (Every D is C), it produces from the fourth conjunction of this figure a particular negative which is the very sought. And because in all, if you judge by C on B, either on some of it or all of it. If on some of it, then some C is some B, so whatever is judged on all B, negatively or affirmatively, enters into it some C which is some B. And if it is on all B, then the judgment by A on some B or all B, affirmative or negative, also enters into it some C, necessarily because there is no B except it is C. And upon these proofs, the conclusion is with special absolute if the premise is from the special absolutes. In summary, the conclusion follows the pure premise in absoluteness and modality by whatever consideration.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth conjunction of the fourth figure, which has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, resulting in a particular negative conclusion. The author gives the example: Some B is C, No B is A, therefore Some C is not A. He then provides three proofs: (1) by contradiction, assuming the opposite 'Every C is A' leads to 'Some B is A' which contradicts 'No B is A'; (2) by conversion of the minor, which turns the mood into the fourth conjunction of the first figure (a known valid mood) and directly gives the conclusion; (3) by assumption (al-iftirad), where we assume a specific individual D that is B and not A, then deduce that D is C, then using a first figure syllogism we get that D is not A, and combining yields Some C is not A. The author then explains the general principle: if the judgment on B by C applies to all or part, then the conclusion about C follows accordingly. He emphasizes that the conclusion's modality (absolute, special, etc.) mirrors that of the premises.

Proof by Assumption (al-Iftirad) in the Fifth Conjunction
Author provides the proof by assumption for the fifth conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the proof by assumption to validate a syllogism in the fifth conjunction.

Core Idea
By assuming a specific individual, we can break down a complex syllogism into simpler first-figure steps.

The author provides a third proof for the fifth conjunction using the method of assumption (iftirad). This proof involves assuming a particular individual D that is B and not A, then using two first-figure syllogisms to derive the conclusion. This technique is powerful for demonstrating validity in complex moods.

Guided Path
1
Assume a specific individual D
Assume D is the part of B that is not A. Then it follows that Every D is B and Every D is C (since all B is C? Wait, the minor is Some B is C, not all. The assumption is that D is precisely the part of B that is both in the minor and not A. Actually, the author says 'assume the part of B which is not A as a specific thing'. Then from 'Some B is C' we get that D is C only if D is that part. The author claims 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C' follow. This is valid if D is a subset of B and a subset of C.
This step uses the fact that from 'Some B is C' we can instantiate to an individual that is both B and C.
2
First syllogism: Every D is B, No B is A
Using Every D is B as minor and No B is A as major, we get No D is A (first figure, Celarent).
This is valid because D is a subset of B.
3
Second syllogism: Every D is C, No D is A
Using Every D is C as minor and No D is A as major, we get Some C is not A (fourth figure, Fresison).
This is exactly the mood we are proving, but with D in place of B. The syllogism is valid.

General Rule for Conclusion from Mixed Premises
Author discusses how the conclusion's modality follows when premises are mixed (absolute, modal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of a syllogism's conclusion when its premises have different modalities (mixed premises), specifically for mixtures of absolute propositions.

Core Idea
In a mixed syllogism, the conclusion inherits the weaker modality.

When the two premises of a syllogism have different modalities—such as one absolute and one modal, or one general absolute and one specific absolute—the conclusion does not automatically take the stronger one. Instead, it follows a rule that depends on the types involved. This lesson examines the rule for mixtures of absolute propositions, which lays the foundation for handling mixtures involving modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Modalities of the Premises
Look at each premise and determine whether it is absolute (general or special) or modal (necessary, possible, etc.). In the example, the major is a specific absolute ('every J exists') and the minor is a general absolute ('every B is A').
The general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) does not specify a time; the special absolute (al-mutlaqa al-khasa) specifies existence at a particular time.
2
Classify the Type of Mixture
The passage categorizes mixtures: between absolutes, between modals, or between absolutes and modals. Here we focus on the first type.
3
Apply the Weaker-Modality Rule
When the mixture is between absolutes, the conclusion takes the weaker modality. Since the general absolute is weaker than the specific absolute, if at least one premise is general absolute, the conclusion will be general absolute.
In the example, the minor is general absolute (weaker), so the conclusion is general absolute.
4
Verify with the Example
From the text: major 'Every J exists' (specific) and minor 'Every B is A' (general absolute) yield 'Some J is A' (general absolute). This confirms the rule.
The conclusion is particular because the syllogism is in the first figure with a particular conclusion format.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرينة مختلطة فالنتيجة على ما فصلناه في الشكل الأول. ثم الاختلاط كما عرف إما بين المطلقات بعضها مع بعض، وإما بين الموجهات، وإما بين المطلقات والموجهات، فإن كان الاختلاط بین المطلقات فإما أن تكون العامة كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كانت كبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل "ج" بالوجود) و(كل "ب" "أ" بالإطلاق العام فـ (بعض "ج" "أ" بالإطلاق العام
And if the premise is mixed, then the conclusion is as we detailed in the first figure. Then mixture, as known, is either between absolutes among themselves, or between modal propositions, or between absolutes and modals. If the mixture is between absolutes, then either the general absolute is major or minor. If it is major, as our saying in the first conjunction: (Every J exists) and (Every B is A in the general absolute form), so (Some J is A in the general absolute form).

Commentary
This passage transitions to discussing cases where the premises have different modalities (mixed). The author reminds that the conclusion follows what was explained earlier in the first figure. He categorizes mixture into three types: (1) between absolute propositions (general absolute and special absolute), (2) between modal propositions (necessary, possible, etc.), and (3) between absolutes and modals. He then begins explaining the first case: if the mixture is between absolutes, the general absolute may be the major or minor. If it is the major, as in the example: the major is 'Every J exists' (a specific absolute) and the minor is 'Every B is A in the general absolute sense', then the conclusion is 'Some J is A in the general absolute sense'. The text cuts off here, but the pattern is that the weaker modality (general absolute) dominates.

Identifying the Fifth Mood of the First Figure
Defines the fifth mood and gives its standard form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood of the first figure by its premise quantities and qualities and construct a valid example.

Core Idea
The fifth mood pairs a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major to yield a particular negative conclusion.

So far we have studied moods with universal premises. The fifth mood introduces a particular minor premise, which forces the conclusion to be particular. This mood is essential for handling arguments where only some members of a category are involved. The passage defines the mood and then demonstrates its validity through multiple logical techniques.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor terms
In the example 'Some B is C' and 'No B is A', the minor term is C (subject of conclusion), the major term is A (predicate of conclusion), and the middle term is B. Label them in your own example.
The minor premise connects B and C; the major premise connects B and A.
2
Check the quantity and quality of each premise
The minor premise 'Some B is C' is particular affirmative. The major premise 'No B is A' is universal negative. These are fixed for the fifth mood.
Any deviation would change the mood.
3
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion 'Some C is not A' must be particular negative. Notice that it follows necessarily: if some B is C, and no B is A, then that part of B which is C cannot be A.
The conclusion cannot be universal because the minor is particular.
4
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Example: 'Some animals are not capable of speech' is not our mood. Use: 'Some humans are prophets. No prophet is a disbeliever. Therefore, some humans are not disbelievers.' This matches the fifth mood.
Ensure the example is valid and terms are clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة ونتيجته جزئية سالبة كقولنا: (بعض ب ج) و(لا شيء من ب أ) فـ (بعض ج ليس أ) وإلا ف (دائماً كل ج أ) وكان (بعض ب ج) فـ (دائماً بعض ب أ) وكان بالإطلاق لا شيء من ب أ) وهو خلف، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب. وإن بينت بالافتراض فرضت البعض من ب شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً د فيلزم (كل د ب) و(كل د ج) فـ (كل د ب) إذا جعل صغرى للكبرى أنتج من الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول (لا شيء من د أ) فإذا جعل هذا اللازم كبرى للازم الآخر وهو (كل د ج) أنتج من الاقتران الرابع من هذا الشكل جزئية سالبة عين المطلوب، ولأنك في الكل إذا حكمت بـ ج على ب فإما على بعضه أو كله، فإن كان على بعضه فبعض ج هو بعض ب فما حكم به على كل ب سلباً كان أو إيجاباً يدخل فيه بعض ج الذي هو بعض ب وإن كان على كل ب فالحكم بـ أ على بعض ب أو كل ب إيجاباً أو سلباً يدخل أيضاً فيه بعض ج ضرورة أنه لا ب إلا وهو ج وعلى هذه البيانات فالنتيجة تكون بالإطلاق الخاص إن كانت القرينة من المطلقات الخاصة، وبالجملة فالنتيجة تابعة للقرينة الصرفة في الإطلاق والجهة بأي اعتبار كان.
The fifth mood: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, its result is a particular negative, as we say: 'Some B is C' and 'No B is A' so 'Some C is not A'. Otherwise, (if) always every C is A, and some B is C, then always some B is A, and (we said) 'No B is A', which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, the mood reverts to the fourth mood of the first figure, producing exactly the required conclusion. And if you prove by assumption, you assume a particular of B to be a definite thing, e.g. D, then it follows that 'Every D is B' and 'Every D is C', so 'Every D is B' if made minor for the major produces from the second mood of the first figure 'Nothing of D is A'. Then if this consequent is made major for the other consequent, which is 'Every D is C', it produces from the fourth mood of this figure a particular negative, exactly the required conclusion. And because in universal terms, when you predicate C of B, either on some or on all. If on some, then some C is some B, so whatever is predicated on all B, negatively or positively, includes that some C which is some B. And if on all B, then the judgment by A on some B or all B, positively or negatively, also includes some C necessarily since there is no B that is not C. And on these proofs, the result is with particular absoluteness if the premise is from the particular absolutes. And in summary, the result follows the pure premise in absoluteness and modality in whatever consideration.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth mood of the first figure of conjunctive syllogism. The author begins by stating its form: a particular affirmative minor premise (some B is C) and a universal negative major premise (no B is A) yield a particular negative conclusion (some C is not A). He then provides a proof by contradiction (خلف): if the conclusion were false, then necessarily all C is A, but since some B is C, then some B is A, which contradicts the major premise. Alternatively, he shows that converting the minor (some B is C becomes some C is B) changes the mood into the fourth mood of the first figure, which directly gives the required conclusion. He also gives a proof by assumption (افتراض): assume a specific individual D that is B; then D is both B and C. From the major premise (no B is A), we get no D is A. Then combining with D is C, we get the particular negative conclusion. The author then explains the underlying reasoning: since the minor is particular, the predication of C covers part of B, and whatever applies to all B applies to that part. Finally, he notes that the conclusion's modality depends on the premises: if the premises are particularly absolute (مطلقات خاصة), the conclusion is particularly absolute; in general, the conclusion follows the modality of the premises.

Key Terms
الاقتران الخامس
جزئية صغرى موجبة
كليّة كبرى سالبة
نتيجة جزئية سالبة

Checkpoint

A) Minor universal affirmative, major universal negative
B) Minor particular affirmative, major universal negative
C) Minor particular negative, major particular affirmative
D) Minor universal negative, major universal affirmative

Key Takeaway
The fifth mood of the first figure has a particular affirmative minor, a universal negative major, and yields a particular negative conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof Methods for the Fifth Mood
Describes three proofs for the fifth mood's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply three proof techniques (reduction to fourth mood, proof by contradiction, proof by assumption) to validate a fifth mood syllogism.

Core Idea
The fifth mood's validity can be shown by converting the minor, assuming the contrary, or assuming a specific instance.

The author does not merely state the fifth mood; he provides robust proofs of its validity. These techniques—reduction, contradiction, and assumption—are essential tools in logical argumentation. Understanding them strengthens your ability to check the validity of any syllogism, especially those with particular premises.

Guided Path
1
Understand reduction by conversion
Convert the minor premise 'Some B is C' to 'Some C is B' (valid immediate inference). Now the premises become: minor 'Some C is B' and major 'No B is A'. This is the fourth mood of the first figure (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major, conclusion particular negative? Actually, the fourth mood in the author's system has a particular negative conclusion – but the fourth mood here is likely the one that yields a particular negative directly. Check: The author says 'عاد الاقتران إلى الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب', meaning it produces exactly the required conclusion. So converting the minor shifts to a mood that already gives the desired conclusion.
Conversion works only for particular affirmative (conversio per accidens).
2
Follow the proof by contradiction (خلف)
Assume the conclusion is false, i.e., 'All C is A' (since its opposite is 'Some C is not A'). From 'Some B is C' and 'All C is A', we deduce 'Some B is A' (by first figure mood with universal major). But this contradicts the major premise 'No B is A'. Therefore the original conclusion must be true.
This is a classical reductio ad absurdum.
3
Work through the proof by assumption (افتراض)
Select any individual D that is B. Then we have 'All D is B' and 'All D is C'. From 'All D is B' and the major 'No B is A', we get 'No D is A' (second mood of first figure: universal affirmative minor, universal negative major → universal negative conclusion). Then combine 'All D is C' and 'No D is A' to get 'Some C is not A' (fourth mood of first figure: universal affirmative minor, universal negative major → particular negative conclusion).
This uses two syllogisms: first to derive that D is not A, then to derive that some C is not A.

Mixing Absolute Premises in the First Figure
Author explains mixing of absolute premises in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion when mixing general and particular absolute premises in the first figure.

Core Idea
Conclusion modality depends on which premise is major and which is minor.

This passage explains how mixing absolute (non-modal) premises affects the conclusion in the first figure. The author distinguishes general absolute (true at all times) from particular/temporal absolute (true at a specific time) and shows that the role of each premise determines the result. This is essential for constructing valid syllogisms with mixed modalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of absolute propositions
Recognize the two subdivisions: general absolute (الإطلاق العام) true at all times, and particular absolute (الإطلاق الخاص or الوقتية) true at a specific time.
2
Determine which premise is major and which is minor
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor. In the examples, identify which premise is general absolute and which is existential or temporal.
3
Apply the rule for each case
Follow the author's derivation: when the major is general absolute and the minor existential, the conclusion is general absolute. When the minor is general absolute and the major existential, the conclusion is particular possibility. When the major is temporal absolute, the conclusion is temporal absolute.
4
Verify using conversion or proof by contradiction
Use conversion of the minor to a different mood, or assume the opposite and derive a contradiction, to confirm the conclusion's modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرينة مختلطة فالنتيجة على ما فصلناه في الشكل الأول. ثم الاختلاط كما عرف إما بين المطلقات بعضها مع بعض، وإما بين الموجهات، وإما بين المطلقات والموجهات، فإن كان الاختلاط بین المطلقات فإما أن تكون العامة كبرى أو صغرى، فإن كانت كبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل ج بالوجود) و(كل ب أ بالإطلاق العام فـ (بعض ج أ بالإطلاق العام وبيانه بما سبق في المطلقات، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى عليك الاعتبار في باقي الاقترانات، وكذا إن كان اختلاطها مع الأخصية وهي كبرى، وإن كانت صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل ب ج بالإطلاق العام) و(كل ب أ بالوجود) فالنتيجة (بعض ج أ بالإمكان الخاص لأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول، والصغرى فيه ممكنة عامة والكبرى وجودية، وقد عرف في ما مضى أن نتيجته ممكنة خاصة، ولأنه لو لم يصدق (بعض ج أ بالإمكان الخاص لصدق (كل ج أ بالضرورة) أو (لا شيء من ج أ بالضرورة) وكان (كل ب ج بالإطلاق العام فيلزمه إما بالضرورة لا شيء من ب أ) وكان بالوجود كل ب أ) وهو خلف. وأما إن كانت المطلقة الأخصيّة أي الوقتية هي الكبرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالإطلاق العام كل ب ج) و (بالإطلاق الوقتي كل ب أ) فـ (بالإطلاق الوقتي بعض ج أ) وبيانه بعكس الصغرى وبالخلف أيضاً، واعتبره في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الموجهات فإما من الضرورية المطلقة أو المشروطة، وإما منها ومن الممكنة، فإن كان من الضرورية المطلقة والمشروطة فإما أن تكون المشروطة مشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع، أو بشرط مع المحمول، أو بوقت، فإن كانت مشروطة بشرط مع الموضوع فإما أن تكون من القسم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، وعلى كل تقدير فإن كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى فالنتيجة ضرورية، والبيان في كل اقتران على ما سبق في الشكل الأول في المطلقات من العكس والخلف والافتراض. وإن كانت صغرى والمشروطة هي الكبرى فإما أن تكون من القسم الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كانت من القسم الأول كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل ب ج بالضرورة) و(كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو أ ما دام ب) فالنتيجة (بعض ج أ بالإمكان العام لأنا إذا قلنا كل ب ج) معناه كل ما
And if the premise is mixed, the result is according to what we detailed in the first figure. Then mixing, as known, is either between absolute propositions with each other, or between modal propositions, or between absolute and modal. If the mixing is between absolute propositions, then either the general (absolute) is major or minor. If it is major, as we say in the first mood: 'Every C exists' and 'Every B is A with general absoluteness' so 'Some C is A with general absoluteness'. And its explanation is according to what preceded in the absolutes. And based on this, the consideration in the remaining moods is not hidden from you. Likewise if its mixing is with the particular (absolute) and it is major. And if it is minor, as we say in the first mood: 'Every B is C with general absoluteness' and 'Every B is A with existence' so the result is 'Some C is A with particular possibility' because if you convert the minor, it returns to the third mood of the first figure, and the minor in it is general possible and the major is existential, and it has been known in what preceded that its result is particular possible. And because if 'Some C is A with particular possibility' is not true, then either 'Every C is A necessarily' or 'No C is A necessarily' would be true, and 'Every B is C with general absoluteness' would entail either 'No B is A necessarily' and there was 'Every B is A with existence', which is a contradiction. And if the particular (absolute), i.e., the temporal absolute, is the major, as we say in the first mood: with general absoluteness 'Every B is C' and with temporal absoluteness 'Every B is A', so (with temporal absoluteness) 'Some C is A'. And its explanation is by conversion of the minor and also by contradiction. And consider this in the remaining moods. And if the mixing is between modal propositions, then either from absolute necessary or conditional necessary, or from them and from possible. If it is from absolute necessary and conditional necessary, then either the conditional necessary is with a condition concerning the subject, or with a condition concerning the predicate, or with a time. If it is conditional with a condition concerning the subject, then it is either from the first division or the second or the third or the fourth. And in every case, if the necessary is the major, the result is necessary. And the proof in each mood is according to what preceded in the first figure regarding the absolutes from conversion, contradiction, and assumption. And if it is minor and the conditional is the major, then it is either from the first division or the second or the third or the fourth. If it is from the first division, as we say in the first mood: 'Every B is C necessarily' and 'Everything that is B always as long as the subject is A as long as B', so the result is 'Some C is A with general possibility' because if we say 'Every B is C' means everything that is...

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixing premises with different modalities in the first figure. The author categorizes mixing into three types: between absolute propositions (مطلقات), between modal propositions (موجهات), and between absolute and modal. He then details each case. For mixing between absolutes, he explains the effects when the general absolute (العامة) is major or minor, using examples from the first mood. He shows that if the major is general absolute and the minor is existential, the conclusion is general absolute. If the minor is general absolute and the major is existential, the conclusion is particular possible (الإمكان الخاص). He provides a proof by contradiction and by conversion. He also discusses the case where the major is a temporal absolute. Then he turns to mixing between modal propositions, specifically necessary and conditional necessary. He outlines the divisions of the conditional necessary (مشروطة) and states that if the necessary is major, the conclusion is necessary; if the necessary is minor and the conditional is major, the conclusion depends on the division. The text cuts off mid-explanation.

الإطلاق العام
الإطلاق الوقتي

Mixing Necessary and Conditional Necessary Premises
Author explains mixing of modal premises (necessary and conditional necessary).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the conclusion's modality when a necessary premise is mixed with a conditional necessary premise in the first figure.

Core Idea
When necessary is major, conclusion is necessary; when necessary is minor and conditional is major, conclusion is possible.

The author now turns to mixing between modal propositions, specifically necessary (الضرورية) and conditional necessary (المشروطة). He outlines that if the necessary is major, the conclusion is necessary; if the necessary is minor and the conditional is major, the result is a possible proposition. He also begins to subdivide the conditional necessary into types based on the condition, though the text cuts off before completing the explanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modal proposition types
Necessary propositions (الضرورية) are true at all times. Conditional necessary (المشروطة) are true as long as the subject exists, possibly with a condition on subject, predicate, or time.
2
Determine which premise is major and which is minor
In the examples, note whether the necessary premise is the major or minor predicate. The author first considers necessary as major, then necessary as minor.
3
Apply the rule for the major being necessary
If the necessary is the major, the conclusion is necessary, regardless of the type of conditional major. The proof uses conversion, contradiction, and assumption as for absolutes.
4
Apply the rule for the minor being necessary and conditional major
If the necessary is minor and the conditional is major, the conclusion is general possible (الإمكان العام). The text provides an example from the first mood with a conditional of the first division.
The text cuts off, but the rule is clear.

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixing premises with different modalities in the first figure. The author categorizes mixing into three types: between absolute propositions (مطلقات), between modal propositions (موجهات), and between absolute and modal. He then details each case. For mixing between absolutes, he explains the effects when the general absolute (العامة) is major or minor, using examples from the first mood. He shows that if the major is general absolute and the minor is existential, the conclusion is general absolute. If the minor is general absolute and the major is existential, the conclusion is particular possible (الإمكان الخاص). He provides a proof by contradiction and by conversion. He also discusses the case where the major is a temporal absolute. Then he turns to mixing between modal propositions, specifically necessary and conditional necessary. He outlines the divisions of the conditional necessary (مشروطة) and states that if the necessary is major, the conclusion is necessary; if the necessary is minor and the conditional is major, the conclusion depends on the division. The text cuts off mid-explanation.

Modal Syllogism: Absolute General Major, Existential Minor
Author explains conclusion of mixed syllogism via conversion and reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion of a first-figure modal syllogism with an absolute general major and an existential minor, and verify it using conversion and reductio.

Core Idea
Mixing absolute premises yields a particular possible conclusion.

Modal syllogisms involve premises with different modal qualifiers. This passage demonstrates how a major premise with absolute generality (al-ṭlāq al-ʿāmm) and a minor premise with existential (al-wujūdī) combine in the first figure to yield a conclusion with special possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ). The author provides two proofs: one by conversion, one by reductio. Understanding this helps you evaluate modal arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and their modalities
In the syllogism, the major premise is 'every B is C' with absolute generality (true in actuality without condition). The minor premise is 'every B is A' with existential (true at some time). Note the difference: absolute generality is unconditional actuality, existential is at least one instance.
2
Recognize the claimed conclusion
The author states the conclusion is 'some C is A' with special possibility – meaning it is possible but not necessary. This is a particular affirmative modal proposition.
Special possibility is the opposite of necessity.
3
Follow the conversion proof
The minor premise 'every B is A' (existential) is converted by simple conversion to 'some A is B' (existential). This new premise, combined with the original major, yields a known valid mood (third conjunction of first figure) whose conclusion is 'some C is A' with special possibility.
Conversion preserves the existential modality.
4
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is false: either 'every C is A' by necessity or 'no C is A' by necessity. Combine these with the major 'every B is C' (absolute general) to derive either 'no B is A' by necessity or 'every B is A' by necessity. Both contradict the minor that 'every B is A' is only existential, not necessary. Thus the original conclusion must hold.
Reductio shows necessity denies the existential minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى عليك الاعتبار في باقي الاقترانات، وكذا إن كان اختلاطها مع الأخصية وهي كبرى، وإن كانت صغرى كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق العام) و(كل "ب" "أ" بالوجود) فالنتيجة (بعض "ج" "أ" بالإمكان الخاص لأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الشكل الأول، والصغرى فيه ممكنة عامة والكبرى وجودية، وقد عرف في ما مضى أن نتيجته ممكنة خاصة، ولأنه لو لم يصدق (بعض "ج" "أ" بالإمكان الخاص لصدق (كل "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) أو (لا شيء من "ج" "أ" بالضرورة) وكان (كل "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق العام فيلزمه إما بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وكان بالوجود كل "ب" "أ") وهو خلف.
And on this basis, it is not hidden from you the consideration in the remaining conjunctions. Similarly, if its mixture is with the more specific (i.e. existential) and it is major, and if it is minor, as we say in the first conjunction: every B is C by absolute generality, and every B is A by existence, so the conclusion: some C is A by special possibility, because if you convert the minor, it returns to the third conjunction of the first figure, and the minor in it is general possibility and the major is existential, and it has been known previously that its conclusion is special possibility; and because if 'some C is A by special possibility' were not true, then 'every C is A by necessity' or 'no C is A by necessity' would be true, and 'every B is C by absolute generality' would necessitate either by necessity 'no B is A' or 'every B is A by existence', which is a contradiction.

Commentary
This passage explains a modal syllogism in the first figure where the major premise is absolute general (al-iṭlāq al-ʿāmm) and the minor is absolute existential (al-wujūdī). The author shows that the conclusion is particular with special possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ). The proof uses conversion of the minor premise and reduction to the third mood of the first figure, which yields a conclusion with special possibility. Additionally, a reductio argument (khulf) is given: assuming the denial of the conclusion leads to necessity and contradiction with the premises.

Mixed Syllogism: Time-Bound Absolute as Major
Author explains conclusion when time-bound absolute is major with general absolute minor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the valid conclusion when the major premise is a time-bound absolute and the minor is a general absolute in the first figure, and verify it by conversion or reductio.

Core Idea
Time-bound major forces a time-bound, particular conclusion.

So far we have seen syllogisms where both premises share the same absolute modality. Now we turn to mixed syllogisms, where one premise is time-bound and the other general. In the previous case, the minor was time-bound and the major general, which yielded a universal time-bound conclusion. Here we swap: the major is time-bound, the minor general. How does the conclusion change? The author shows that it becomes particular time-bound. Understanding this distinction is crucial when applied to juristic reasoning where temporal restrictions matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The major premise is time-bound absolute: 'Every B is A at time t.' The minor is general absolute: 'Every B is C.' Note the order: major = time-bound, minor = general. This matches the first figure with B as the middle term.
The passage uses 'first conjunction' meaning first figure.
2
Recognize the conclusion's modality
Since the major is time-bound, the conclusion must also be time-bound; but because the minor is general (not time-bound), the conclusion cannot be universal—it must be particular. So the conclusion is 'Some C is A at time t.'
General absolute allows no restriction, so the conclusion inherits the time-boundedness of the major but only partially.
3
Prove by conversion of the minor
Convert the minor 'Every B is C' to 'Some C is B' (accidental conversion of universal affirmative to particular). Now we have: major 'Every B is A (time-bound)' and minor 'Some C is B'. By first figure (Darii), we get 'Some C is A (time-bound)'.
This is the method the author refers to as 'by conversion of the minor'.
4
Prove by reductio
Assume the opposite of the conclusion, i.e., 'No C is A (time-bound)' or 'Some C is not A (time-bound)'. Combine with premises to derive a contradiction, showing the assumption false. The author mentions this alternative proof.
Reductio is often used to confirm validity when direct proof is cumbersome.
5
Apply to other figures
The author says 'consider it in the remaining conjunctions,' meaning the same reasoning applies to the second, third, and fourth figures when the major is time-bound and the minor general. The process of conversion or reductio will yield analogous valid conclusions.
This shows the principle is figure-independent; the modality relationship is key.

The conclusion is a particular time-bound absolute. The proof uses conversion of the minor premise and a reductio argument, similar to the previous case. The author instructs the reader to apply the same reasoning to the other conjunctions (figures/moods).

Mixed Modal Syllogisms: Necessary and Conditional
Author categorizes mixtures of necessary absolute and conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of conditional proposition in a mixed modal syllogism and determine the conclusion's modality when the necessary proposition is the major premise.

Core Idea
When necessity meets condition, the major premise dictates the conclusion's modality.

In the previous lessons, we learned about modal propositions and syllogisms composed entirely of absolute propositions. Now, the author introduces mixtures of modal propositions, specifically combining necessary absolute (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa) with conditional (mashrūṭa) propositions. He categorizes the types of conditional propositions and gives a key rule about the conclusion's modality when the necessary proposition serves as the major premise. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating theological and juristic arguments that mix necessary and conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate the necessary absolute proposition and the conditional proposition in the syllogism. The necessary absolute proposition is true necessarily in actuality (e.g., 'Every A is necessarily B'). The conditional proposition holds under a condition (e.g., 'Every C is B as long as it is D').
2
Classify the conditional proposition
Determine whether the condition is attached to the subject, the predicate, or a time. The author mentions three possibilities: condition on the subject, condition on the predicate, or condition on a time. If the condition is on the subject, further identify which of the four divisions it belongs to (as per earlier classification, though not detailed here).
The four divisions likely refer to degrees of constancy or temporality of the condition.
3
Check which premise is the major
Identify which premise is the major (the one containing the predicate of the conclusion) and which is the minor. According to the author, if the necessary absolute proposition is the major premise, then the conclusion will be necessary.
4
Apply the rule
If the necessary proposition is major, the conclusion is necessary. If it is not major, the conclusion's modality may differ, but the passage does not specify that case.
The author states: 'وإن كانت الضرورية هي الكبرى فالنتيجة ضرورية' (if the necessary is the major, the conclusion is necessary).
5
Verify using proof methods
The author notes that the demonstration for each conjunction follows the same methods as in the first figure with absolute propositions: conversion, reductio ad absurdum, and supposition (ifḍāḍ). You can test the validity of the mixed syllogism using these tools.
These proof methods are taught in earlier lessons on the first figure.

And in every case, if the necessary is the major, the conclusion is necessary; and the demonstration in each conjunction is as preceded in the first figure regarding absolutes, by conversion, reductio, and supposition.

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of mixed syllogisms involving modal propositions, specifically necessary absolute (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa) and conditional (mashrūṭa) propositions. The author divides conditional propositions into those with a condition on the subject, on the predicate, or on time. Additionally, those with a condition on the subject are further divided into four sections (presumably based on the nature of the condition). The key rule stated is: when the necessary proposition is the major premise, the conclusion is necessary. The proof methods for each conjunction (figure/mood) are the same as previously discussed for absolute propositions: conversion, reductio, and hypothesis (ifḍāḍ).

The Syllogism

10 min
Necessary minor, conditional major (first section)
Author explains conclusion when necessary is minor and conditional (first section) is major

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conclusion and perform a reductio proof for a syllogism with a necessary minor and a conditional major of the first division.

Core Idea
When the minor is necessary and the major a constant-time conditional, the conclusion is particular and modally general possible.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq addresses a mixed modal syllogism: the minor is necessary universal affirmative, and the major is a conditional of the first division (every B always as long as B exists is A as long as B exists). The author derives the conclusion 'some C is A by general possibility' and provides both a direct and a reductio proof. Understanding this case helps you handle modal nuances in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The minor is 'كل ب ج بالضرورة' (every B is C by necessity). The major is 'كل ما هو ب دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو أ ما دام ب' (everything that is B always as long as the subject persists is A as long as B persists), which is the first division of the conditional.
Note that the major is a universal conditional with a temporal persistence condition.
2
Determine the conclusion's quantity
The author states the conclusion is 'بعض ج أ بالإمكان العام' (some C is A by general possibility). It is particular because not all C are B; only those C that are always B fall under the major premise.
The conclusion is not necessary but general possible, because the A attribute may be necessary or possible.
3
Follow the direct proof
From 'كل ب ج', everything described by B is C, including the subset that is always B. These are the Bs that 'دائماً بدوام الموضوع'. They fall under the major, so they are A. Since that subset is some C, we get 'بعض ج أ'. The mode is 'بالإمكان العام' because the major's A could be necessary or possible.
This shows how the conditional major restricts the application.
4
Understand the reductio proof
The author argues: if the conclusion were false, then 'لا شيء من ج أ بالضرورة' (no C is A by necessity) would be true. Together with the minor 'كل ب ج بالضرورة', we get 'لا شيء من ب أ بالضرورة' (no B is A by necessity). But this contradicts the major which states 'كل ب أ دائماً' (every B is A always). Hence the conclusion must hold.
This is a classic reductio, showing the necessity of the conclusion's truth.
5
Extend to other conjunctions
The author says 'واعتبر في ما بقي من الاقترانات' (and consider it in the remaining conjunctions), meaning the same reasoning applies to other figures and moods of mixed modal syllogisms.
This is an invitation to generalise the pattern.

And if it is minor and the conditional is the major, then it is either of the first, second, third, or fourth division. Commentary
This passage addresses the case where the minor premise is necessary and the major is a conditional of the first division (i.e., 'every B always as long as B exists is A as long as B exists'). The conclusion is 'some C is A by general possibility'. The author provides a detailed proof: from the minor 'every B is C', we know that whatever is described by B is C, including those Bs that are always B as long as they exist. However, not all C are B, so some C are B always, and these fall under the major premise, so they are A. But since the major premise is conditional, the A attribute may be necessary or possible, and the common possibility is general possibility. Then a reductio argument is given: if the conclusion were false, then 'no C is A' would be necessary, and with 'every B is C' necessary, we would get 'no B is A' necessary, contradicting the major premise. The author generalizes this to other conjunctions.

Deriving the Conclusion with a Second-Division Conditional Major
Author explains conclusion when conditional major is of the second division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a first-figure syllogism when the major premise is a second-division conditional and the minor is a universal necessary categorical.

Core Idea
When the major is second-division, the conclusion inherits the temporal restriction from the major.

In this short passage, the author demonstrates how to handle a syllogism where the minor premise is a universal necessary categorical ('every B is C by necessity') and the major premise is a second-division conditional ('everything that is B is A at some times, not always'). Understanding this pattern is crucial for correctly concluding when a predicate applies only sometimes to the subject. The key is that the temporal limitation transfers from the major to the conclusion because the minor identifies a subset of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Minor Premise
Locate the minor premise: 'every B is C by necessity'. Note that it is a universal affirmative with necessity. The subject is B, predicate C.
This is the first conjunction (first figure) as the author specifies.
2
Identify the Major Premise
The major premise is a second-division conditional: 'everything that is B always as long as the subject persists is A at some times of its being B, not always'. This means that for any B, A holds true only at some times, not permanently.
Second division: always B but A only at some times.
3
Determine the Figure and Middle Term
The middle term is B (appears in both premises). The minor term is C (subject of conclusion) and the major term is A (predicate of conclusion). This is the first figure (middle term subject of major, predicate of minor).
The author explicitly says 'in the first conjunction'.
4
Derive the Conclusion
Since the minor says that every C is actually a B (because every B is C? Wait, minor says every B is C, so all B are C. That means C is the predicate of the minor, so the subject of the conclusion is C? Actually in first figure, minor: B is C, major: A is B, conclusion: A is C? Standard first figure: major: A is B, minor: C is A, conclusion: C is B. But here the author uses 'every B is C by necessity' as the minor and 'everything that is B is A at some times' as the major. So the middle term is B, major term is A, minor term is C. The conclusion should be 'some C is A at some times' because A holds for B only at some times, and C is a subset of B (since every B is C, so C is larger? Actually if every B is C, then B is a subset of C. So C includes all B and possibly more. But the major says A holds for everything that is B only at some times. For the C that are not B, we don't know if A holds. So the conclusion must be limited to 'some C is A at some times', specifically those C that are B. The author concludes 'some C is A at some times, not always'.
The temporal restriction from the major carries over because the minor establishes that all instances of B are C, so any C that is also B inherits the A property at some times.
5
Generalize to Other Conjunctions
The author states that the same pattern applies to the remaining conjunctions (figures), with appropriate adjustments for the positions of terms.
This is a generalization step for completeness.

Syllogism with Fourth-Division Conditional Major
Author explains conclusion when conditional major is of the fourth division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of a syllogism where the minor is a necessary universal and the major is a fourth-division conditional proposition.

Core Idea
The fourth-division conditional yields a particular temporal conclusion.

This lesson builds on the previous treatment of the third division of conditional propositions. Here we examine the fourth division, where the major premise states that whatever is B is A at some times but not as long as B persists. Combined with a necessary universal minor, we must carefully track the temporal qualifications to reach the correct conclusion. The author provides the result and invites the reader to verify and generalize.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
The minor is 'every B is C by necessity'. This means that whenever B occurs, C is present necessarily, without temporal restriction.
The minor is universal and necessary.
2
Identify the major premise
The major is from the fourth division: 'everything that is B not always as long as the subject persists is A at some times of its being B, not as long as B persists'. This means that for any B, it is A only at some moments when it is B, and not continuously throughout its existence.
The major is a conditional categorical: it adds a temporal condition to the predicate.
3
Combine the premises to derive the conclusion
From the minor, any C that comes from B will share the temporal behavior of B. Since the major says that B is A only at some times, the conclusion about C must also be limited: 'some C is A at some times, not always'. The quantifier 'some' captures that only those C that are B at the relevant times are A.
The conclusion is particular and temporal because the major is not universal in time.
4
Verify using the previous explanation
The author says the verification is the same as the preceding explanation (the third division). In the third division, the conditional had the form 'every B is A at some times, not always as long as B persists', and the conclusion was similarly particular. The fourth division differs in the exact temporal condition but the reasoning pattern is analogous.
Generalize this method to other conjunctions (e.g., second, third, fourth figures) by substituting the terms appropriately.

Particular Necessary Propositions with Conditionals
Author states rule about particular necessary with conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a particular necessary proposition cannot produce a conclusion when mixed with a conditional that has a condition on the subject.

Core Idea
A particular necessary premise is too weak to combine with conditionals.

Here the author states a crucial restriction: if a necessary premise is particular (i.e., 'some B is necessarily C'), mixing it with a conditional that imposes a condition on the subject yields no valid conclusion. This rule is essential for evaluating mixed syllogisms in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of necessary proposition
Determine if the necessary premise is universal (all) or particular (some). The rule applies when it is particular.
Particular necessary: like 'some bodies are necessarily created'.
2
Identify the conditional type
The conditional must have a condition on the subject (sharṭ maʿa al-mawḍūʿ). Recognize that such conditionals are not simple categoricals but express a relationship.
Example: 'If it is a body, it is temporally originated.'
3
Apply the rule
If the necessary is particular and mixed with such a conditional, no conclusion follows. The author says 'fā lā intāj ʿanhā' (there is no production from it).
This applies whether the necessary is the minor or major premise.
4
Test with a concrete example
Construct an invalid mixed syllogism to see why it fails. Try using a particular necessary premise and a conditional, and check that no valid conclusion emerges.
Example: 'Some bodies are necessarily created. If it is a body, it changes. Therefore...' No valid conclusion.

And know that whenever the absolute necessary is particular, there is no production from it, if it is mixed with any of the conditionals that have a condition on the subject, regardless of whether the necessary is minor or
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage states an important rule: if the necessary premise (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa) is particular (juz’iyya) — i.e., an 'some' proposition — then no valid conclusion (intāj) can be produced when it is mixed with any conditional proposition that has a condition on the subject (sharṭ maʿa al-mawḍūʿ). This rule applies whether the necessary premise is the minor or the major. The text cuts off, but the rule is clear.

Mixing Necessary with Three Types of Conditional Premises
Author explains the results of mixing necessary major with three types of conditional minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the modality of the conclusion when a necessary major is combined with a conditional minor of type 2, 3, or 4 in the first figure.

Core Idea
The conclusion's modality follows the weaker conditional premise, not the necessary major.

Modal syllogisms in Islamic logic often combine premises with different temporal modalities. This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq teaches how to determine the conclusion when a necessary major premise is paired with a conditional minor premise of one of three types. We will walk through each case step by step using the author's examples with variables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal character of the minor premise
For each type, note whether the conditional premise asserts that the predicate holds 'always by virtue of the subject' (type 2: in some times, not always), 'as long as the subject exists' (type 3), or 'in some times, not as long as it exists' (type 4). This determines the temporal condition that will transfer to the conclusion.
2
Recognize the overlap from the necessary major
The necessary major 'Every B is necessarily J' implies that the set B is fully contained in J, but also that every B is J necessarily. This allows us to say that some J are B (since B is not empty). The minor premise applies only to those things that are B, so its condition applies to that subset of J.
3
Transfer the condition from B to the subset of J
Because some J are B, the temporal condition stated in the minor (for B) applies to that part of J. The conclusion will be about 'some J' with the same temporal modality as the minor, but only for that specific instance.
4
Articulate the conclusion's modality
For type 2: conclusion is 'Some J is A in some times, not always'. For type 3: conclusion is 'Some J is A at some time' (indefinite). For type 4: conclusion is 'Some J is A in some times, not always'. The key is that the conclusion never reaches necessity because the minor is conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت المشروطة هي القسم الثاني كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل "ب" "ج" بالضرورة) و(كل ما هو "ب" دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ب" لا دائماً) فالنتيجة (بعض "ج" "أ" في بعض الأوقات لا دائماً) لأن "أ" ثابت كذلك، و"ج" هو بعض "ب" فكان له حكمه، وعلى هذا في سائر الاقترانات. وأما إن كانت المشروطة هي القسم الثالث كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" ما دام "ب") فالنتيجة (بعض "ج" "أ" في وقت ما لأنا إذا قلنا كل "ب" "ج") كان معناه كل ما هو "ب" كيف كان دائماً للموضوع، أو غير دائم فإنه بالضرورة "ج" ويدخل فيه ما "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "ج"، فبعض ما يقال له "ج" هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع، فيدخل تحت قولنا: (وكل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع "أ" لا دائماً واعتبر في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كانت المشروطة من القسم الرابع كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل) "ب" "ج" بالضرورة) و(كل ما هو "ب" لا دائماً بدوام الموضوع فهو "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ب" لا ما دام "ب"، فالنتيجة (بعض "ج" "أ" في بعض الأوقات لا دائماً) وتحقيقه بالبيان الذي قبله واعتبره في ما بقي من الاقترانات.
And if the conditional is the second type, like our saying in the first figure: (Every B is necessarily J) and (Everything that is B always by virtue of the subject is A in some of the times it is B, not always), then the result is (Some J is A in some times, not always) because A is established likewise, and J is some B, so it has its ruling, and likewise in the remaining figures. And if the conditional is the third type, like our saying in the first figure: (Every B is necessarily J) and (Everything that is B not always by virtue of the subject is A as long as it is B), then the result is (Some J is A at some time) because when we say 'Every B is J', its meaning is that everything that is B, whether always for the subject or not, is necessarily J, and includes what is B not always by virtue of the subject, so it is J. So some of what is called J is B not always by virtue of the subject, and thus falls under our saying: (And everything that is B not always by virtue of the subject is A not always). And consider the remaining figures accordingly. And if the conditional is of the fourth type, like our saying in the first figure: (Every B is necessarily J) and (Everything that is B not always by virtue of the subject is A in some of the times it is B, not as long as it is B), then the result is (Some J is A in some times, not always). Its verification is by the preceding explanation, and consider it in the remaining figures.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage defines the results of mixing a necessary major premise with a conditional minor premise in the first figure of categorical syllogism. The author considers three types of conditional propositions: the second type where the predicate holds for the subject in some times but not always; the third type where the predicate holds as long as the subject exists; and the fourth type where the predicate holds in some times but not as long as the subject exists. For each type, the conclusion's modality is derived: for the second type, the conclusion is particular and holds in some times; for the third type, the conclusion is particular and holds at some indefinite time; for the fourth type, the conclusion is particular and holds in some times but not always. The explanation relies on the fact that the middle term (B) partially overlaps with the minor term (J) due to the necessary premise, so the conclusion follows the modality of the conditional premise. All examples use logical variables (ج، ب، أ) and the figures are treated generally.

When a Particular Necessary Premise Fails to Produce a Conclusion
Author declares that a particular necessary premise mixed with a conditional premise yields no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a particular necessary premise mixed with a conditional premise fails to produce a syllogistic conclusion and explain why the middle term is not identical.

Core Idea
A particular necessary premise breaks the syllogism because the middle term may not be the same in both premises.

In this passage, the author explains a subtle but critical rule: a particular necessary premise (e.g., 'Some B is necessarily J') cannot combine with a conditional premise (e.g., 'Every B that is C is D') to yield a conclusion. The reason lies in the potential mismatch of the middle term's extension. Understanding this condition prevents false inferences in logic, especially when dealing with modal propositions in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify premise types
Determine the type of each premise. The first is a particular necessary proposition (e.g., 'Some B is necessarily J'). The second is a conditional proposition that imposes a condition on the subject (e.g., 'Every B that is C is D').
The conditional premise may be universal or particular, but it restricts the subject to those meeting the condition.
2
Check the middle term's quantification
The middle term is B. In the particular necessary premise, only some B is J. In the conditional premise, only those B that satisfy the condition are considered. The part of B that is J (from the first premise) may not be the same as the part of B that satisfies the condition (from the second premise).
The middle term B is not distributed in either premise when particular, so its extension may differ.
3
Determine if the middle term is identical
For a valid categorical syllogism, the middle term must be the same in both premises. Here, it is possible that the particular B from the first premise is not included in the conditional B from the second premise. Thus, they may not refer to the same individuals.
The author's phrase 'the part of B that is J is not the B that is judged by the major term' highlights this mismatch.
4
Conclude that no inference is possible
Since the middle term is not guaranteed to be identical, no necessary connection exists between the major and minor terms. Therefore, no conclusion follows from these premises.
This rule applies whether the necessary premise is minor or major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه مهما كانت الضرورية المطلقة جزئية فلا إنتاج عنها، إذا كانت مختلطة بشيء من المشروطات بشرط مع الموضوع، وسواء كانت الضرورية صغرى أو كبرى لجواز اختلاف الحد الأوسط، فإنه من الجائز أن يكون البعض من "ب" الذي هو "ج" ليس هو "ب" المحكوم عليه بالحد الأكبر، فما التقيا.
And know that whenever the absolute necessary proposition is particular, there is no production from it when mixed with any of the conditional propositions with a condition of the subject, whether the necessary is minor or major, because the middle term may differ. For it is possible that the part of B that is J is not the B that is judged by the major term, so they do not meet.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage warns that a particular necessary premise (e.g., 'Some B is necessarily J') cannot yield a conclusion when combined with a conditional premise that imposes a condition on the subject. The reason is that the middle term (B) is not universally quantified: the part of B that is J (from the minor premise) may not be the same part of B that receives the attribute from the conditional premise. Thus, there is no common subject to link the major and minor terms, and no syllogistic inference is possible.

Mixed Syllogisms with Predicate and Time Conditions
Author explains mixed syllogisms when conditional is major with predicate condition or time condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion in a mixed syllogism where a necessary minor is combined with a conditional major under a predicate or time condition, using conversion and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
In mixed syllogisms, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise.

This passage from Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq examines how to combine a necessary premise with a conditional premise under specific conditions. The author shows that when the necessary premise is minor and the conditional is major, the conclusion takes the modality of the major—whether it be a predicate condition or a time condition. You will learn to prove the conclusion using conversion and contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Recognize that the syllogism is initially not in the first figure, but after conversion it becomes first figure. Locate the minor (necessary) and major (conditional) premises.
The original form has the middle term as subject in both premises.
2
Apply conversion to the minor
Convert the necessary minor premise: from 'Every B is J' to 'Some J is B'.
Conversion of a universal affirmative yields a particular affirmative.
3
Reconstruct the syllogism in the first figure
After conversion, the minor becomes 'Some J is B' and the major remains 'Every B is A (condition)'. This is now a first figure syllogism.
First figure: major premise has middle as subject, minor has middle as predicate.
4
Determine the conclusion's modality
The conclusion follows the modality of the major, so it is conditional: 'Some J is A (condition)'.
The conclusion is particular affirmative with the same condition as the major.
5
Validate via contradiction (khalf)
Assume the opposite conclusion (e.g., 'No J is A as long as A'), combine with the minor, and derive a contradiction (e.g., 'No B is A as long as A'), which is false.
This reductio confirms the original conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية والمشروطة بشرط المحمول أو بشرط وقت فالنتيجة أيضاً تابعة لجهة الكبرى، أما إذا كانت الضرورية كبرى فللبيان السابق في اختلاطها مع المشروطات بشرط الموضوع. وأما إن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى فإن كانت المشروطة بشرط مع المحمول كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(كل "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ") فالنتيجة (بعض "ج" "أ" ما دام "أ" لأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الأول، والكبرى فيه مشروطة بشرط المحمول، واتباع النتيجة فيه لجهة الكبرى بين، ولأنه لو لم يصدق (بعض "ج" "أ" ما دام "أ") وإلا لصدق (لا شيء من "ج" "أ" ما دام "أ" وكان (كل) "ب" "ج") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "أ") وهو خلف. وإن كانت المشروطة مشروطة بوقت فإما أن يكون الوقت معيناً أو غير معين فإن كان معيناً كقولنا في الاقتران الأول (كل) قمر فهو جرم سماوي بالضرورة)، و(كل قمر فهو ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة)، فـ (بعض الأجرام السماوية ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة)، وإلا فـ (لا شيء من الأجرام السماوية ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة)، وكان بالضرورة كل قمر فهو جرم سماوي) فـ ( لا شيء من القمر ينخسف في الوقت المذكور) وهو خلف، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول، والكبرى كبراه واتباع النتيجة لها فيه بين. وأما إن كان الوقت غير معين كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة كل إنسان ناطق)، و(كل إنسان فهو متنفس في وقت غير معين، فـ بعض الناطق متنفس في وقت غير معين وإلا فـ دائماً لا شيء من الناطق متنفس في وقت غير معين، وكان كل إنسان متنفس في وقت غير معين فـ دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان متنفس في وقت غير معين
And if the mixing is between the necessary and the conditional under condition of the predicate or condition of time, the result also follows the modality of the major. As for if the necessary is major, the explanation is previous in its mixing with conditionals under condition of the subject. And if it is minor and the conditional is major, then if the conditional is under condition with the predicate, like our saying in the first figure: (Every B is necessarily J) and (Every B is A as long as it is A), then the result is (Some J is A as long as A) because if you convert the minor, it returns to the first figure, and the major therein is conditional under condition of the predicate, and the following of the result to the modality of the major is clear. And because if (Some J is A as long as A) were not true, then (No J is A as long as A) would be true, and (Every B is J) so (No B is A as long as A), which is a contradiction. And if the conditional is conditioned by time, then it is either specified or unspecified. If specified, like our saying in the first figure: (Every moon is a celestial body necessarily) and (Every moon is eclipsed at the time of its opposition to the sun at the node), then (Some celestial bodies are eclipsed at the time of their opposition to the sun at the node). Otherwise, (No celestial body is eclipsed at the time of its opposition to the sun at the node) and (Every moon is a celestial body necessarily) so (No moon is eclipsed at that time), which is a contradiction. And because if you convert the minor, it returns to the third figure of the first, and its major is its major, and the following of the result to it is clear. And if the time is unspecified, like our saying in the first figure: (Every human is rational necessarily) and (Every human is breathing at an unspecified time), then (Some rational is breathing at an unspecified time). Otherwise, always (No rational is breathing at an unspecified time) and (Every human is breathing at an unspecified time), so always (No human is breathing at an unspecified time), which is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses mixed syllogisms where the necessary premise is minor and the conditional premise is major, under two types of conditions: predicate condition and time condition. First, when the conditional major is under predicate condition (e.g., 'Every B is A as long as it is A'), the result follows the modality of the major, yielding 'Some J is A as long as A'. The proof uses conversion (aks) of the minor to restore the first figure. If the conclusion were false, a contradiction arises. Second, when the conditional major is under time condition, two subcases arise: specified time (e.g., lunar eclipse at a precise time) and unspecified time (e.g., breathing at some time). In both, the conclusion is particular and follows the major's modality, proven by conversion and contradiction. The examples are from Islamic astronomy (lunar eclipse) and human nature (breathing), illustrating the logical forms.
