Mixing Necessary and Conditional Premises in Syllogisms
Author gives rules and examples for mixing necessary and conditional premises in modal syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the conclusion follows the major premise's modality in modal syllogisms mixing necessary and conditional premises, and verify the conclusion using conversion and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
In modal syllogisms, the conclusion's modality is determined by the major premise, not the minor.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq expands on the rules for mixing necessary (ḍarūriyya) and conditional (mashrūṭa) premises. You will learn the general principle and see worked examples with proofs via conversion and reductio. Understanding this is crucial for evaluating arguments in kalam that combine necessary truths with contingent conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major and minor premises
In a first-figure syllogism, the major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion. Determine the modality of each premise: necessary (ḍarūriyya) or conditional (mashrūṭa) with condition of predicate or time.
The minor premise is the one containing the subject of the conclusion.
2
Apply the general rule: conclusion follows the major's modality
If the major is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if the major is conditional, the conclusion is conditional with the same condition. This is the author's key principle.
3
Test subcase 1: conditional with condition of predicate
Take 'Necessarily, every B is J' (minor) and 'Every B is A as long as it is A' (major). Convert the minor to 'Some J is B necessarily'. Then the syllogism becomes first figure with conditional major, so conclusion 'Some J is A as long as it is A'. Verify via reductio.
The proof assumes the negation and derives a contradiction using the premises.
4
Test subcase 2: conditional with definite time
Example: 'Every moon is a celestial body necessarily' (minor) and 'Every moon is eclipsed at a specific time' (major). Conclusion: 'Some celestial bodies are eclipsed at that time'. Proof via reductio: if false, then no celestial body is eclipsed at that time, contradicting the minor.
5
Test subcase 3: conditional with indefinite time
Example: 'Every human is rational necessarily' (minor) and 'Every human is breathing at an indefinite time' (major). Conclusion: 'Some rational beings are breathing at an indefinite time'. Proof via conversion: convert the minor to 'Some rational is human', then use third figure.
The same rule applies whether time is definite or indefinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية والمشروطة بشرط المحمول أو بشرط وقت فالنتيجة أيضاً تابعة لجهة الكبرى، أما إذا كانت الضرورية كبرى فللبيان السابق في اختلاطها مع المشروطات بشرط الموضوع. وأما إن كانت صغرى والمشروطة كبرى فإن كانت المشروطة بشرط مع المحمول كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة كل ب ج وكل ب أ ما دام أ فالنتيجة بعض ج أ ما دام أ لأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الأول، والكبرى فيه مشروطة بشرط المحمول، واتباع النتيجة فيه لجهة الكبرى بين، ولأنه لو لم يصدق بعض ج أ ما دام أ وإلا لصدق لا شيء من ج أ ما دام أ وكان كل ب ج فـ لا شيء من ب أ ما دام أ وهو خلف. وإن كانت المشروطة مشروطة بوقت فإما أن يكون الوقت معيناً أو غير معين فإن كان معيناً كقولنا في الاقتران الأول كل قمر فهو جرم سماوي بالضرورة، وكل قمر فهو ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة، فبعض الأجرام السماوية ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة، وإلا فـ لا شيء من الأجرام السماوية ينخسف في وقت مقابلته للشمس في العقدة، وكان بالضرورة كل قمر فهو جرم سماوي فـ لا شيء من القمر ينخسف في الوقت المذكور وهو خلف، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول، والكبرى كبراه واتباع النتيجة لها فيه بين. وأما إن كان الوقت غير معين كقولنا في الاقتران الأول بالضرورة كل إنسان ناطق، وكل إنسان فهو متنفس في وقت غير معين، فبعض الناطق متنفس في وقت غير معين وإلا فـ دائماً لا شيء من الناطق متنفس في وقت غير معين، وكان كل إنسان متنفس في وقت غير معين فـ دائماً لا شيء من الإنسان متنفس في وقت غير معين وهو خلف، ولأنه بعكس الصغرى يرتد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وعلى هذا فاعتبره في ما بقي من الاقترانات بالعكس والخلف، وسواء كانت الضرورية صغرى أو كبرى.
And as for if the mixing is between the necessary and the conditional with the condition of the predicate or with the condition of time, then the conclusion also follows the aspect of the major premise. As for if the necessary is the major, then [recourse is] to the previous explanation in its mixing with conditionals with the condition of the subject. And as for if it is the minor and the conditional is the major, then if the conditional is with a condition of the predicate, like our saying in the first figure: 'Necessarily, every B is J' and 'Every B is A as long as it is A', then the conclusion is 'Some J is A as long as it is A'. That is because if you convert the minor, it returns to the first figure, and the major in it is conditional with the condition of the predicate, and the following of the conclusion to the aspect of the major in it is clear. And because if it were not true that 'Some J is A as long as it is A', then otherwise 'No J is A as long as it is A' would be true, and 'Every B is J', so 'No B is A as long as it is A', and that is a reductio. And if the conditional is conditional with time, then either the time is definite or indefinite. If it is definite, like our saying in the first figure: 'Every moon is a celestial body necessarily' and 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of its opposition to the sun at the node', then 'Some celestial bodies are eclipsed at the time of their opposition to the sun at the node'. Otherwise, 'No celestial body is eclipsed at the time of its opposition to the sun at the node' and 'Necessarily, every moon is a celestial body', so 'No moon is eclipsed at the mentioned time', and that is a reductio. And because if you convert the minor, it returns to the third figure of the first, and the major [is as] its major, and the following of the conclusion to it in it is clear. And as for if the time is indefinite, like our saying in the first figure: 'Necessarily, every human is rational' and 'Every human is breathing at an indefinite time', then 'Some rational is breathing at an indefinite time'. Otherwise, 'Always, no rational is breathing at an indefinite time' and 'Every human is breathing at an indefinite time', so 'Always, no human is breathing at an indefinite time', and that is a reductio. And because by converting the minor it returns to the third figure of the first, producing exactly the desired conclusion. And upon this, consider it in the remaining figures by conversion and reductio, whether the necessary is minor or major.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules for mixing necessary (ḍarūriyya) and conditional (mashrūṭa) premises in modal syllogisms. The author states a general principle: when mixing necessary and conditional premises, the conclusion follows the modality of the major premise. The passage then provides detailed proofs for the case where the necessary premise is minor and the conditional is major, using examples from the first figure. Three subcases are covered: (1) conditional with condition of predicate, (2) conditional with definite time, and (3) conditional with indefinite time. For each, the author demonstrates that the conclusion shares the modality of the major premise, and proves it via conversion (`aks) and reductio ad absurdum (khulf). For example, with the moon and eclipse (subcase 2), if the major is conditional with a definite time, the conclusion is also conditional with that time. The proof involves assuming the contrary, converting the minor premise, and showing a contradiction. The passage concludes by generalizing that this method applies to all syllogistic figures.

Mixing Necessary and Possible Premises in Modal Syllogisms
Author states the rule for mixing modals and the exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for mixing necessary and possible premises in modal syllogisms, identify the exception, and construct a reductio proof.

Core Idea
The conclusion follows the possible premise—except in the sixth figure with a universal negative.

Modal syllogisms involve premises of different modalities, like necessity and possibility. This passage presents a general rule and an important exception. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where modal reasoning is used to establish certainties and possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modalities of the premises
Determine if each premise is necessary (ḍarūriyya) or possible (mumkina). The possible premise can be in the general, special, or most special sense.
The passage does not define these senses but treats them uniformly for the rule.
2
Apply the general rule
If one premise is possible, the conclusion must be possible, not necessary, regardless of which premise (major or minor) is the possible one.
This is stated as uncontroversial: 'there is no disagreement that the conclusion follows it.'
3
Check for the exception
The exception is when the possible premise is a universal negative in the 'special sense' and appears in the 'sixth figure' (a figure that reduces to the second figure of the first). In this case, there is disagreement.
The author does not explain the disagreement but says it is 'similar to the disagreement about what reduces to the second figure of the first.'
4
Understand the proof method
The author gives a reductio proof: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine it with the minor premise, and you will derive the contradictory of the major premise. This shows the original conclusion follows. Also, conversion and supposition (iftirāḍ) can be used.
This proof method is a standard technique in modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية والممكنة فإن كانت الممكنة فلا خلاف في اتباع النتيجة لها كانت ممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، إلا أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى الخاص كليّة سالبة كما في الاقتران السادس فالخلاف فيه على نحو الخلاف في ما يرتد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الأول، كما عرف، ولأنك مهما أخذت نقيض النتيجة وجعلته كبرى للصغرى في الكل أنتج المقدمة الكبرى، وهو خلف وعليك باعتبار العكس والافتراض، إن رُمت البيان به في ما يتبين به. وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المشروطة والممكنة فالحكم فيها على ما عرف في الشكل الأول، وبأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة والممكنة، وأيهما كانت كبرى أو صغرى والبيان من الخلف والعكس والافتراض فكما علم.
And as for if the mixing is between the necessary and the possible, then if the possible [premise] is [so], there is no disagreement that the conclusion follows it, whether the possible is in the general sense, the special sense, or the most special sense, except if the possible in the special sense is a universal negative, as in the sixth figure; then the disagreement about it is similar to the disagreement about what reduces to the second figure of the first, as is known. And because whenever you take the contradictory of the conclusion and make it a major for the minor in all [cases], it produces the major premise, and that is a reductio. And you must consider conversion and supposition if you intend its explanation in what will be explained. And as for if the mixing is between the necessary conditional and the possible, then the ruling is as known in the first figure, and by whatever consideration the conditional and the possible are, and whichever of them is major or minor, and the explanation by reductio, conversion, and supposition is as known.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixing necessary (ḍarūriyya) and possible (mumkina) premises in modal syllogisms. The author states a general principle: when one premise is possible, the conclusion follows the modality of that possible premise, regardless of whether the possible is understood in a general, special, or most special sense. However, there is an exception: when the possible premise is a universal negative in the 'special sense' (al-maʿnā al-khāṣṣ) and occurs in the sixth figure (which reduces to the second figure of the first), there is disagreement. The author then provides a proof method using reductio: assume the contradictory of the conclusion, combine it with the minor premise, and it will produce the major premise as a conclusion, leading to a contradiction. Conversion and supposition (iftirāḍ) are also mentioned as alternative methods. The passage then briefly covers mixing necessary-conditional (ḍarūriyya mashrūṭa) with possible premises, referring back to the rules of the first figure and the same proof techniques.

Key Terms
ممكنة
الاقتران السادس
افتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The necessary premise
B) The possible premise
C) The major premise
D) The minor premise

Key Takeaway
When mixing necessary and possible premises, the conclusion takes the modality of the possible premise, except for a universal negative possible in the sixth figure, which has disagreement.

The Three Always-Productive Conditional Mixes
Author lists three productive conditional types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conditional premises that always produce a valid conclusion when mixed.

Core Idea
First three conditional mixes always yield conclusions; only the fourth type has exceptions.

In this section, the author of Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq introduces the rules for mixing conditional premises. He first lists three types that invariably produce a valid conclusion: (1) mixing from the genus of ascription of the subject to that with which it is placed, (2) mixing from the conditional with condition of the predicate, and (3) mixing from the conditional with condition of time. Understanding these types is foundational for constructing sound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first three types
The author lists three specific types of conditional premises: (1) mixing from the genus of ascription of the subject to that with which it is placed (ittiṣāf al-mawḍūʿ bimā wuḍiʿa maʿahu), (2) mixing from the conditional with condition of the predicate (mashrūṭa bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl), and (3) mixing from the conditional with condition of time (mashrūṭa bi-sharṭ waqt).
These three types are always productive.
2
Understand the significance of production
Production (intāj) means that a valid conclusion necessarily follows from the premises. For these three types, whenever two premises of the same type are mixed, a conclusion is generated.
3
Apply to an example from kalam
Consider the conditional: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a cause.' This is a conditional with condition of the predicate (type 2). Mixing it with another premise of the same type (e.g., 'If it has a cause, then the cause is external') yields a valid syllogism.
This shows how the rule is used in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المشروطات بعضها مع بعض فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه، ومن المشروطة بشرط المحمول والمشروط بشرط وقت، أو من أقسام المشروطة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه بعضها مع بعض، فإن كان الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في اتباع النتيجة وفي البيان له بطريقة فكما سلف. وإن كان الرابع وهو اختلاط بين أقسام المشروطة بشرط الموضوع فلا إنتاج إن كان الاختلاط من أحد القسمين الأولين، وأحد القسمين الآخرين ولا تبالي أي قسم كان منها، إذ الحكم في القسمين الأولين على ما الشرط فيه دائم بدوام الموضوع، وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط بين الطرفين يكون مختلفاً، بل إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الأول والثاني أو الثالث والرابع فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في النتيجة والبيان فكما سبق في ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض فالحكم أيضاً في الإنتاج
And as for if the mixing is among conditionals with each other, then either the mixing is from the genus of the ascription of the subject to that with which it is placed, and from the conditional with condition of the predicate and the conditional with condition of time, or from the divisions of the conditional from the genus of the ascription of the subject to that with which it is placed among themselves. If it is the first, second, or third, then production occurs, and the ruling concerning the conclusion's following and the explanation for it by way of [the method] is as previously. And if it is the fourth, which is mixing between the divisions of the conditional with condition of the subject, then there is no production if the mixing is between one of the first two divisions and one of the other two, and it does not matter which division it is, because the ruling in the first two divisions is that the condition is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and at that point the middle term between the two extremes becomes different. Rather, if the mixing is between the first and second, or the third and fourth, then production occurs, and the ruling concerning the conclusion and the explanation is as previously when the minor was necessary. And as for if the mixing is among possibles with each other, then the ruling is also concerning production.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of mixing conditional premises (mashrūṭāt) among themselves. The author identifies four types: (1) mixing from the genus of the ascription of the subject to that with which it is placed, (2) mixing from the conditional with condition of the predicate, (3) mixing from the conditional with condition of time, and (4) mixing between divisions of the conditional with condition of the subject. The first three types always produce valid syllogisms (intāj wāqiʿ), with the conclusion following the same rules as previously discussed. The fourth type does not produce a valid conclusion when mixing one of the first two divisions (which have permanent conditions) with one of the other two divisions, because the middle term becomes different (i.e., not the same in both premises). However, mixing within the same subgroup (e.g., first with second, or third with fourth) does produce a valid conclusion, following the same rules as when the minor is necessary. The passage ends by noting that mixing possible premises among themselves also has rules for production, but the text cuts off here.

Key Terms
اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه
مشروطة بشرط المحمول
مشروط بشرط وقت
إنتاج

C) Mixing from condition of the predicate and subject
D) Mixing from divisions of the ascription of the subject

Key Takeaway
The first three types of conditional premises always yield a valid conclusion when mixed.

advanced
6 min
The Fourth Type: Condition of Subject
Author explains the fourth type and its mixing rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when mixing conditionals with condition of the subject produces a valid conclusion and when it does not.

Core Idea
Mixing conditionals of the subject type fails across subgroups but succeeds within the same subgroup.

The fourth type of conditional mixing involves the condition of the subject (mashrūṭa bi-sharṭ al-mawḍūʿ). The author explains that this type is subdivided, and mixing across these subdivisions does not produce a conclusion, while mixing within the same subdivision does. This lesson dissects this rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth type and its subdivisions
The fourth type is mixing between divisions of the conditional with condition of the subject. These divisions consist of two subgroups: the first two divisions (where the condition is permanent with the subject) and the other two divisions.
The author calls them 'the first two divisions' and 'the other two divisions' – these are internal to the fourth type.
2
Determine when production fails
If the mixing is between one premise from the first two divisions and one premise from the other two divisions, no production occurs. The reason: the middle term becomes different (i.e., the condition in one premise is permanent, while in the other it is not), so the middle term does not remain the same across premises.
3
Determine when production succeeds
If the mixing is within the same subgroup – either both from the first two divisions (first with second) or both from the other two divisions (third with fourth) – then production occurs. The rule for the conclusion follows that of when the minor premise is necessary.
The author references an earlier rule about necessary minors.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider: (Premise 1) 'If the traveler is present (condition of subject), he must complete the prayer' – this is a permanent condition. (Premise 2) 'If the traveler is present, he may not shorten' – also permanent. Mixing these (both from first two) gives a valid conclusion. But if Premise 2 had a different condition linked to time (non-permanent), mixing would fail.
This mirrors the author's rule about the middle term becoming different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for if the mixing is among possibles with each other, then the ruling is also concerning production.

Mixing Possible Premises
Author mentions mixing possibles among themselves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize that mixing possible premises among themselves also follows rules for production, and identify the need to consult further texts for detailed rules.

Core Idea
Possible premises also have production rules, though the text cuts off before detailing them.

The author ends this section by addressing the mixing of possible (mumkināt) premises among themselves. He states that 'the ruling is also concerning production,' indicating that similar rules apply. However, the text cuts off before explaining them. This lesson highlights the existence of these rules and points to where they are developed in later works.

Guided Path
1
Locate the reference to possibles
The author writes: 'And as for if the mixing is among possibles with each other, then the ruling is also concerning production.' This is a brief mention, likely to be followed by further explanation in the original text.
2
Understand the context
The previous sections covered necessary and conditional premises; now possibles (those that could be or not be) are considered. The author implies that similar principles of mixing apply.
Possible premises include statements like 'It is possible that Zayd is standing'.
3
Consult later works
For the detailed rules, one must refer to later parts of Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq or other logic texts like al-Qiyās by al-Fārābī or commentaries by al-Taftāzānī.

General Rule for Mixing Necessary and Possible
Author states the rule for modal conclusion in mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of the conclusion in a mixed modal syllogism involving necessary and possible propositions, and identify the exception for universal negative possible propositions in the sixth figure.

Core Idea
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion follows the possible, not the necessary.

When combining necessary and possible propositions, one might assume the conclusion follows the stronger modality (necessary). However, the logicians established a different rule. Let's examine the passage where the author clarifies this principle and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about conclusion modality
Find the statement that the conclusion follows the possible proposition (الممكنة). The author says 'لا خلاف في اتباع النتيجة لها'.
2
Note the three subtypes of possible mentioned
The possible can be general (المعنى العام), specific (المعنى الخاص), or most specific (المعنى الأخص). The rule applies to all three.
3
Locate the exception clause
The exception occurs when the possible is a universal negative in the specific sense in the sixth figure. Then there is disagreement analogous to a known case in the second figure.
The author says 'فالخلاف فيه على نحو الخلاف في ما يرتد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الأول'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت غیر معين وهو خلف، ولأنه بعكس الصغرى يرتد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وعلى هذا فاعتبره في ما بقي من الاقترانات بالعكس والخلف، وسواء كانت الضرورية صغرى أو كبرى. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية والممكنة فإن كانت الممكنة فلا خلاف في اتباع النتيجة لها كانت ممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، إلا أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى الخاص كليّة سالبة كما في الاقتران السادس فالخلاف فيه على نحو الخلاف في ما يرتد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الأول، كما ،عرف، ولأنك مهما أخذت نقيض النتيجة وجعلته كبرى للصغرى في الكل أنتج المقدمة الكبرى، وهو خلف وعليك باعتبار العكس والافتراض، إن رمت البيان به في ما يتبين به.
An indefinite time, and it is a reductio argument, and because by converting the minor premise it returns to the third figure from the first, producing the very sought conclusion; so consider this in the remaining figures by conversion and reductio, whether the necessary proposition is minor or major. As for mixing of the necessary and the possible: if the possible is [the one present], there is no disagreement that the conclusion follows it, whether the possible is in the general sense, the specific sense, or the most specific sense, unless the possible in the specific sense is a universal negative as in the sixth figure, then there is disagreement in the manner of the disagreement regarding what returns to the second figure from the first, as is known; and because whenever you take the contradictory of the conclusion and make it a major premise for the minor premise in all cases, it produces the major premise, which is a reductio; and you must consider inversion and supposition if you desire clarification by what is clarified.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when combining necessary and possible propositions. The author first mentions a reduction method using contraposition (عكس) and reductio ad absurdum (خلف) to reduce any figure to the first figure, applicable whether the necessary proposition is minor or major. Then he addresses the case of mixing necessary and possible. The general rule is that the conclusion follows the modality of the possible proposition, regardless of whether the possible is in the general sense (المعنى العام), specific sense (المعنى الخاص), or most specific sense (المعنى الأخص). An exception occurs in the sixth figure when the possible proposition is a universal negative in the specific sense; then there is disagreement analogous to a known case in the second figure. The author also explains the reductio method: taking the contradictory of the conclusion as a premise to derive the original major premise, and mentions using inversion (عكس) and supposition (افتراض) for clarification. This is a technical discussion of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms

الضرورية
المعنى العام
المعنى الخاص
المعنى الأخص

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms, the conclusion generally takes the modality of the possible premise, except for a specific case in the sixth figure.
